You have access to Keepa's "Sold in 30 Days" metrics for multiple sellers, alongside each seller's 30-day stock history graph. Your task is to identify genuine sales from these graphs by differentiating between actual customer purchases and inventory adjustments or removals.

Important Guidelines:

1. Distinguish Genuine Sales vs. Adjustments
- Small, stepwise declines over several days typically represent real sales.
- Sudden, large single-day drops often indicate stock removal, relocation, or other non-sale adjustments.
- If the graph stays flat after a large drop, assume zero (or near-zero) actual sales occurred during that period.

2. Estimate True Sales
- For each seller, examine the entire 30-day graph.
- Count only the cumulative effect of gradual stepwise decreases as sales.
- Exclude the "big drop" (relatively speaking meaning big drop compared to product approximate daily sales (estimated using monthly sold yellow graph line)) sections from your sales count if they happen in a single day.
- If the line is flat for an extended period, assume zero sales in that time.

3. Compare Against Keepa "Sold 30 Days" (which is found in the "Sold 30 Days" column on the left of the graphs)
- State Keepa's reported sales figure.
- Provide your own best estimate of actual sales based on the graph analysis.
- If there is a major discrepancy (more than 5–10 units difference), explain briefly why (e.g., "One large stock adjustment was misread as sales").

4. Seller-by-Seller Summary
- Include for each seller:
   - Keepa 30-day sold figure.
   - Your estimated 30-day sales.
   - Notable restocks (sharp upward jumps).
   - Notable zero-sales segments (flat lines).
   - Any big drop events that are likely not real sales.
   - Whether the discrepancy is minor or major.

5. Recommendations
- Conclude with any general insights or recommendations (e.g., "Sellers showing large abrupt drops may be inflating their 'Sold 30 Days' figures.").

Output Format:
- Present the final result in a concise, clear table or bullet format.
- Highlight which sellers have consistent keepa-vs.-actual numbers and which do not.

CRITICAL NOTES:
1) Whenever you come across a "?" this is the same as 0, however if the "?" only appears in the buybox stats screenshot, it implies only that the seller is out of stock, which does not necessarily mean that the seller also had 0 sales.

2) UNDER ABSOLUTELY NO CIRCUMSTANCE can your estimated/calculated sales value be higher than sales mentioned in the screenshots (use this when checking for discrepancies after you complete your own sales analysis and calculations), even if value in 30 days sold column is ? (= 0)

3) After completing your analysis for each seller, if discrepancy between Keepa sold in 30 days and yours are major and not due to a sharp drop (in a single day) retrace the graph line to confirm your initial analysis and calculation and if needed, rectify your error.

4) DISCREPANCY CROSS-CHECKING STRATEGY: Check the sum of all the sales you estimated and compare it to the monthly sold value (extracted from the pricing history graph screenshot- containing 3 graphs); it is the value extracted from the yellow line in the middle graph), review your calculations and if needed rectify any misinterpreted/miscalculated seller's 30 day sales metric. Do not compare it with the sum of the "30 day sold" column, it has to be compared with the value extracted from the yellow line in the pricing history screenshot. The value will be the same as the one mentioned on the bottom of the screenshot in the sentence: "Bought in past month:"

HistWeeklyRate = true_30d / 4.3.

Buy Box share formula per week

For all in-stock sellers that week:

Compute four normalized components:

FBA vs FBM (65%)

Weight 1.0 if FBA, 0.3 if FBM.

Normalized across active sellers → gives 65% of BB share.

Price competitiveness (22.5%)

PriceScore = (min_price / seller_price).

Normalized → 22.5% of BB share.

Reviews (5%)

Score ≈ √(review_count) to avoid mega-store domination.

Sellers without stats get a small baseline score.

Normalized → 5% of BB share.

Stock (8.5%)

Proportional to current stock → 8.5% of BB share.

Sum of components = BuyBoxShare_seller (sums to 1.0 across sellers with stock>0).

Weekly sales per seller

For week t:

Sales
week
=
(
HistWeeklyRate
×
𝑤
)
+
(
BuyBoxShare
×
MarketVolume
)
Sales
week
	​

=(HistWeeklyRate×w)+(BuyBoxShare×MarketVolume)

w = 0.3 for Weeks 1–2; w = 0.2 for Weeks 3–7.

If calculated sales > current stock → clamp to stock and mark OOS.

This construction naturally pushes total weekly sales slightly above 69.8 when a lot of stock is available, and below when many sellers run out. I did not force-fit totals back to 69.8; see cross-check later.

7. Recommendations (≤8 bullets)

Enter the listing is acceptable: with 300+ monthly units and your relatively small 60-unit batch, the main risk is not getting any Buy Box exposure. The model suggests you do get a slice even at £11.50, mainly because you’re FBA and many rivals have limited FBA stock.

Price positioning: £11.50 is at the top end of the current FBA range (vs ~£10.74–11.10). If you want a more conservative entry, consider a launch band of £11.29–£11.49, then creep up only if Buy Box holds and sell-through is strong.

Watch REVON + YourEverydayStuff closely: they are the true aggressors (FBM but heavy BB share). If either drops significantly below ~£10.70, expect pressure on the FBA cluster and potential Buy Box compression.

FBA cluster is shallow: current FBA sellers with meaningful stock (Northmax, E-com height, Elteuara, Hexses FBA, The Online Stores FBA) collectively hold small quantities. This is good for you – FBA stock churns quickly, so you don’t need a huge share to sell 60 units.

Big FBM inventories are “parked”: The Online Stores FBM, Hexses FBM, and Shakti have large stocks but low BB influence. They mainly matter in worst-case price-war scenarios or if Amazon shifts more Buy Box to non-Prime.

Monitor real vs modelled weekly totals: if you see the real market selling clearly <70/week for several weeks, downgrade the optimistic side of this forecast and assume your best/worst curves are shifted right by ~1 week.

Exit rule: if Buy Box collapses below ~£10.70 and stays there for more than a week with FBA competition still heavy, you’re too far above breakeven to play that game. Plan in advance whether you’d (a) accept a small loss at a lower price, or (b) dump via other channels.

Re-run with your exact fee inputs: once you lock in the final FBA + referral + prep numbers, re-plug the breakeven; if breakeven turns out higher than 11.10, the tiny £24 gross becomes even thinner and you may prefer a faster liquidation price with minimal loss instead of chasing profit.

You are required to conduct a RIGOROUS COMPUTATIONAL ANALYSIS using advanced forecasting methodologies to predict inventory depletion with maximum accuracy. 

MANDATORY APPROACH:
You MUST generate TWO INDEPENDENT forecasts using different methodologies, then compare and reconcile them into a final forecast.

═══ METHOD 1: MONTE CARLO SIMULATION (REQUIRED) ═══

Implement a Monte Carlo simulation with the following specifications:

1. DEMAND MODEL:
   - Daily demand follows Negative Binomial distribution
   - Mean = WeeklyMarketVolume / 7 (from Phase 1 yellow line)
   - Dispersion parameter k = 1.2 (or estimate from variance if data available)
   - Run 10,000 iterations minimum

2. BUY BOX ALLOCATION (per simulation iteration):
   - Use Dirichlet distribution to model stochastic Buy Box share
   - Alpha parameters built from weighted score for each seller:
       * α_i = w_FBA × FBA_score + w_Price × Price_score + w_Reviews × Review_score + w_Stock × Stock_score
       * Weights: FBA=0.65, Price=0.225, Reviews=0.05, Stock=0.085
   - Price_score = (min_price / seller_price) clipped to [0.80, 1.10]
   - Review_score = (seller_reviews / max_reviews)
   - Stock_score = (seller_stock / total_stock)
   - FBA_score = 1.0 if FBA, 0.3 if FBM
   - Assert: Sum of weights = 1.0

3. DAILY SIMULATION LOOP (for each of 10,000 iterations):
   - For each day in 8-week period:
       a) Draw daily demand from Negative Binomial
       b) Draw Buy Box shares from Dirichlet(alpha)
       c) Allocate demand proportionally to Buy Box shares
       d) Clamp sales to available stock for each seller
       e) Update stock levels
       f) When seller hits stock=0, re-normalize Buy Box shares among remaining sellers
       g) Model restock hazard: λ_i = exp(− stock_i / θ) where θ = 24-30 (case size)

4. OUTPUT FROM MONTE CARLO:
   - Weekly sales statistics: mean, median, 95% confidence intervals
   - Sell-out week probability distribution
   - My Buy Box share over time with confidence bands
   - Revenue and profit distributions

═══ METHOD 2: FACTOR-WEIGHTED DETERMINISTIC MODEL ═══

Implement the factor-weighted approach as described in standard Prompt 3:

WEEKLY SALES FORMULA:
Sales_week = (HistWeeklyRate × w) + (BuyBoxShare × MarketVolume)

where:
- w = 30% in Week 1 & Week 2, 20% in subsequent weeks
- MarketVolume = Monthly Sold ÷ 4.3
- BuyBoxShare calculated using same weights as Monte Carlo (65/22.5/5/8.5)
- Sales clamped to stock; OOS sellers removed and shares re-normalized

Aggregates:

Weekly sales for YOU (8 weeks).

Weekly average Buy Box share for YOU (mean p_tYOU over each week).

Total revenue = (units sold by you) × £11.50.

Total profit = (units sold) × £0.30 (from your VAT-aware calculator).

Total weekly volume (∑ all sellers) vs baseline 69.8.

1.2 Example Python code (you can run this directly)

Below is a simplified but complete Python skeleton you can run in your own environment to generate the Monte Carlo outputs. It matches your specification (no restocks implemented; you can add them if you define a rule).

import numpy as np

# ------------ FIXED INPUTS ------------
MONTHLY_SOLD = 300.0              # from yellow line
WEEKS = 8
DAYS = 7 * WEEKS
MARKET_WEEKLY = MONTHLY_SOLD / 4.3
DAILY_MEAN = MARKET_WEEKLY / 7.0
K = 1.2                            # dispersion

# Per-unit economics
PRICE_YOU = 11.50
PROFIT_PER_UNIT = 0.30            # from your calculator

# Sellers: (name, is_fba, price, reviews, initial_stock, hist_30d_sales_true)
sellers_base = [
    ("REVON LTD",            False, 10.78,  2713,  21,  90),
    ("YourEverydayStuff",    False, 10.75, 35136,  53,  90),
    ("Northmax",             True,  11.99,    26,  10,  76),
    ("The Online Stores FBA",True,  10.99, 30524,  21,   2),
    ("E-com height",         True,  10.74,  4713,   5,  16),
    ("Elteuara",             True,  10.99,    25,   5,  13),
    ("Hexses Outlet FBA",    True,  11.10,   291,   6,   9),
    ("The Online Stores FBM",False, 10.95,   100, 123,   3),
    ("Hexses Outlet FBM",    False, 10.80,   100, 142,   1),
    ("Shakti Housewares",    False, 10.95,   100, 116,   1),
    ("YOU",                  True,  11.50,     0,  60,   0),   # assumption: 60 units
]

# ------------ HELPER FUNCTIONS ------------

def nb_params_from_mean_k(mean, k):
    p = k / (k + mean)
    return int(round(k)), p  # n, p in numpy's negative_binomial

def build_alpha(active_sellers):
    # active_sellers: list of dicts with stock>0
    prices = np.array([s["price"] for s in active_sellers], dtype=float)
    stocks = np.array([s["stock"] for s in active_sellers], dtype=float)
    reviews = np.array([s["reviews"] for s in active_sellers], dtype=float)
    is_fba  = np.array([s["is_fba"] for s in active_sellers], dtype=bool)

    min_price = prices.min()
    price_score = np.clip(min_price / prices, 0.80, 1.10)

    max_reviews = max(reviews.max(), 1.0)
    review_score = reviews / max_reviews

    total_stock = max(stocks.sum(), 1.0)
    stock_score = stocks / total_stock

    fba_score = np.where(is_fba, 1.0, 0.3)

    alpha = (0.65 * fba_score
             + 0.225 * price_score
             + 0.05 * review_score
             + 0.085 * stock_score)
    return alpha

# ------------ MONTE CARLO CORE ------------

def run_monte_carlo(n_iter=10000, seed=42):
    rng = np.random.default_rng(seed)
    n_param, p_param = nb_params_from_mean_k(DAILY_MEAN, K)

    # results containers (YOU=last index in sellers_base)
    idx_you = len(sellers_base) - 1
    weekly_sales_you = np.zeros((n_iter, WEEKS), dtype=float)
    weekly_bb_you    = np.zeros((n_iter, WEEKS), dtype=float)
    sellout_week     = np.full(n_iter, WEEKS+1, dtype=int)  # >WEEKS means not sold out

    for it in range(n_iter):
        # Initialize sellers state
        sellers = []
        for (name, is_fba, price, reviews, stock, hist30) in sellers_base:
            sellers.append({
                "name": name,
                "is_fba": is_fba,
                "price": price,
                "reviews": reviews,
                "stock": float(stock),
                "hist_weekly": hist30 / 4.3,
            })

        you_sold_cum = 0.0
        you_sellout_recorded = False

        # tracking per-day share & sales for YOU
        daily_sales_you = np.zeros(DAYS, dtype=float)
        daily_bb_you    = np.zeros(DAYS, dtype=float)

        for day in range(DAYS):
            active_idx = [i for i, s in enumerate(sellers) if s["stock"] > 0]
            if not active_idx:
                break

            active_sellers = [sellers[i] for i in active_idx]
            alpha = build_alpha(active_sellers)
            # Validation: sum of weights > 0
            assert (alpha > 0).all()

            shares = rng.dirichlet(alpha)
            demand = rng.negative_binomial(n_param, p_param)
            # allocate integer demand
            alloc = rng.multinomial(demand, shares)

            # map back to global indices
            for a_idx, global_i in enumerate(active_idx):
                s = sellers[global_i]
                want = alloc[a_idx]
                sold = min(s["stock"], want)
                s["stock"] -= sold
                if global_i == idx_you:
                    daily_sales_you[day] = sold
                    daily_bb_you[day] = shares[a_idx]
                    you_sold_cum += sold

            # record sellout day for YOU
            if (not you_sellout_recorded) and sellers[idx_you]["stock"] <= 0:
                you_sellout_recorded = True
                week_index = day // 7
                sellout_week[it] = week_index + 1

        # aggregate per week
        for w in range(WEEKS):
            start = w*7
            end   = min((w+1)*7, DAYS)
            weekly_sales_you[it, w] = daily_sales_you[start:end].sum()
            if end > start:
                weekly_bb_you[it, w] = daily_bb_you[start:end].mean()

    return weekly_sales_you, weekly_bb_you, sellout_week

# Example usage (locally, not here):
# weekly_sales_you, weekly_bb_you, sellout_week = run_monte_carlo(10000)

In general:

Deterministic realistic is more of a smoothed central path.

## GLOBAL GRAPH INTERPRETATION RULES (CRITICAL)

When you read the new Keepa screenshots, **you MUST follow these rules** for every seller:

1. **Genuine sales vs adjustments:**
   - Treat **small stepwise declines over several days** as **real customer sales**.
   - Treat **sudden large one-day drops** (relative to typical daily movement from the yellow-line monthly sold) as **inventory movements / removals / relocations**, **not** real sales.
   - If a **big drop** is followed by:
     - A near-mirror **upward jump** → treat both as stock relocation or correction, not sales.
     - A long **flat line** → interpret almost no real sales during that period.
   - Only count **gradual stepwise decreases** as sales in your per-seller true-sales calculation.

2. **Keepa 30-day sold column as hard upper bound:**
   - For each seller:  
     **Under absolutely no circumstance** may your estimated 30-day sales **exceed** the Keepa “Sold (30 days)” number for that seller.
   - If Keepa shows “?” in “Sold (30 days)”, treat that as **0** for the **upper bound**.
   - If “?” appears only in Buy Box stats for stock, it just means they’re currently OOS for Buy Box purposes – it does **not automatically imply** 0 past sales.

3. **Cross-check vs “Bought in past month” (yellow line):**
   - Let **MonthlySold_market** = value from the **yellow line** “Bought in past month” on the main price/volume graph.
   - Let **SumTrueSales_graph** = sum of your per-seller true 30-day sales from the stock graphs (only those with visible stock history).
   - If there are sellers appearing **only** in Buy Box stats (no stock graphs, “?” stock now):
     - For cross-check **only**, you may approximate their 30-day sales as:  
       approx_sales = BuyBox% × MonthlySold_market.
     - **Never** use this approximation in the depletion forecast itself – only for validation.
   - Check:  
     SumTrueSales_graph + SumApprox_missing_sellers should be **close to** MonthlySold_market (±10 units).  
   - If not, **retrace** the graphs to look for misread big drops or miscounted steps. Do **not** force-fit the numbers; explain any persistent gap.

4. **OOS & “?” handling:**
   - If a seller is OOS now and only appears in **Buy Box stats with “?” stock**, **exclude them from the forward forecast**, but include them in the **back-test** of the last month where relevant.
   - Mark any seller or datum you cannot clearly interpret as:  
     UNVERIFIABLE – [brief reason].

5. **Seller behaviour classification (carry over from prior Phase 2):**
   - Re-assess **each in-stock seller** as:
     - **Aggressive BuyBox Chaser** – frequently matches/undercuts Buy Box in ≤1 day.
     - **Reactive BuyBox Chaser** – eventually converges to Buy Box, but with lag/infrequent steps.
     - **Premium (Slight / Moderate / High)** – structurally above Buy Box; rarely chases.
   - Use **price line vs Buy Box line** and updated Buy Box stats (%) to refine your classification.

     - Absolute error.

## PHASE B: METHOD 1 – RECALIBRATED MONTE CARLO SIMULATION

You MUST implement an 8-week **daily Monte Carlo simulation** with at least **10,000 iterations**, now **recalibrated** using the new realized data.

### 1. Demand model (Negative Binomial)

- Let **WeeklyMarketVolume_new** = updated weekly market volume from the yellow line.  
  - Use: WeeklyMarketVolume_new ≈ MonthlySold_market / 4.3.  
- Daily mean demand:  
  - μ = WeeklyMarketVolume_new / 7.
- Dispersion parameter:
  - Target **k = 1.2** by default.  
  - If variance from realized daily/weekly data is inferable, re-estimate k from:  
    Var ≈ μ + μ² / k.  
  - Explain whether you kept k=1.2 or recalibrated.

- Daily demand distribution per iteration:  
  - D_t ~ NegBinomial(mean = μ, dispersion = k).

### 2. Buy Box allocation (Dirichlet per day)

For each seller i **with stock > 0** that day:

- Compute:

  - FBA_score_i = 1.0 if FBA, 0.3 if FBM.
  - Price_score_i = clip(min_price / seller_price_i, 0.80, 1.10).
  - Review_score_i = seller_reviews_i / max_reviews_across_sellers.
  - Stock_score_i = seller_stock_i / total_stock_all_sellers.

- Given fixed weights (assert sum = 1.0):

  - w_FBA = 0.65  
  - w_Price = 0.225  
  - w_Reviews = 0.05  
  - w_Stock = 0.085

- Alpha parameter for Dirichlet:

  - α_i = w_FBA * FBA_score_i + w_Price * Price_score_i + w_Reviews * Review_score_i + w_Stock * Stock_score_i.

Validate:
- α_i > 0 for all i.
- Normalization: sum of α_i is irrelevant for Dirichlet (only relative values matter), but record the vector for diagnostics.

Per day, sample:

- BB_share_vec_t ~ Dirichlet(α_1, α_2, ..., α_n).

### 3. Daily simulation loop (per iteration, 8 weeks)

For each iteration:

- Initialise stocks for each seller, including **my FBA listing** with [INSERT UNITS] at price [INSERT PRICE].
- For each day t in 8 weeks (56 days):

  a. Draw D_t from Negative Binomial.  
  b. Draw BB_share_vec_t from Dirichlet using current α.  
  c. Translate daily demand into per-seller demand:  
     - Demand_i_t = D_t * BB_share_i_t.  
  d. Clamp to stock: Sales_i_t = min(Demand_i_t, Stock_i_t).  
  e. Update stock: Stock_i_t+1 = Stock_i_t − Sales_i_t.  
  f. If Stock_i_t+1 = 0 for seller i, remove them from future BB allocation and **renormalize** α and BB shares among remaining in-stock sellers.  
  g. Restock hazard (basic):  
     - λ_i = exp(− Stock_i_t / θ) with θ in [24, 30] (case size).  
     - If modelling restock, specify whether you actually trigger Poisson restock events or keep restock hazard as diagnostic only. Be explicit.

- Repeat for **≥10,000 iterations**.  
- Run a **convergence check**: repeat with 5,000 iterations and confirm key metrics move by <5%.

### 4. Monte Carlo outputs (required)

For **my listing** and for each week (1–8):

- Weekly sales statistics:
  - Mean, median, 2.5th and 97.5th percentiles (95% CI).
- Weekly Buy Box share:
  - Mean and 95% CI.
- Stock path:
  - Start stock, mean sold, 95% CI sold, end stock each week.
- Sell-out week distribution:
  - P(sell_out_by_week_w) for w = 1..8.

For the **whole market**:

- Total weekly volume vs WeeklyMarketVolume_new baseline:
  - Mean ± 95% CI each week.
- Major seller OOS timing:
  - Probability distribution of week each major competitor goes OOS.

Estimate **Revenue & Profit distributions** for my listing:

- Revenue per iteration = units_sold * my_price.  
- Profit per iteration = units_sold * NetProfit_per_Unit using the input unit economics.  
- Report mean, median, and 95% CI for both.

## PHASE C: METHOD 2 – RECALIBRATED DETERMINISTIC MODEL

Implement the factor-weighted deterministic model as in the original Prompt 3, **but recalibrated** with new data.

### 1. HistWeeklyRate & MarketVolume

- HistWeeklyRate_i = recalculated true 30-day sales_per_seller / 4.3.
- MarketVolume_new = MonthlySold_market / 4.3 (from yellow line).

### 2. Buy Box share (deterministic)

Use the **same factor structure as in Monte Carlo** (FBA/Price/Reviews/Stock with weights 65/22.5/5/8.5), but now as **deterministic scores**:

- For each week:
  - Compute FBA_score_i, Price_score_i, Review_score_i, Stock_score_i from that week’s **starting state**.
  - Convert to BuyBoxShare_i_week via weighted normalized scores.
  - When a seller hits stock=0, remove and renormalize shares for subsequent weeks.

### 3. Weekly sales formula

For each seller i and each week w:

- Sales_week_i = (HistWeeklyRate_i * w_factor) + (BuyBoxShare_i_week * MarketVolume_new)

Where:
- w_factor = 0.30 in Weeks 1–2.
- w_factor = 0.20 in Weeks 3–8.
- Clamp Sales_week_i to stock (no seller sells more than they have).
- When clamped to stock at 0, reallocate their BuyBoxShare proportionally among remaining sellers in following weeks.

### 4. Three deterministic scenarios

Use the **recalibrated inputs** to run:

1. **Best-Case:**
   - Rivals collectively:
     - Raise price **+5%**.
     - Or go OOS **20% sooner** (effectively reducing their starting stock by 20% or shifting depletion earlier).
   - Recompute Price_scores and shares accordingly.
2. **Realistic:**
   - Today’s observed configuration (prices, stock, behaviours) persists.
3. **Worst-Case:**
   - Introduce a **new aggressive FBM rival**:
     - Price at least **3% below** the current cheapest price.
     - Starting stock = **25% of combined existing stock**.
     - Behaviour: Aggressive BuyBox Chaser with strong reviews.
   - Recompute factor scores and Buy Box allocations including this rival.

Produce a single **8-week table** per scenario:

- Columns:  
  Seller | Type | Start | Sold | End | My BB % | Notes
- Include a bottom row: **Total weekly sales** (all sellers), to be checked against MarketVolume_new (±10%).

Compute per scenario:

- Sell-out week for **my listing**.
- Total units sold by me in 8 weeks.
- Total revenue (units_sold * my_price).
- Total profit (units_sold * NetProfit_per_Unit).

## GLOBAL GRAPH INTERPRETATION RULES (CRITICAL)

When you read the new Keepa screenshots, **you MUST follow these rules** for every seller:

1. **Genuine sales vs adjustments:**
   - Treat **small stepwise declines over several days** as **real customer sales**.
   - Treat **sudden large one-day drops** (relative to typical daily movement from the yellow-line monthly sold) as **inventory movements / removals / relocations**, **not** real sales.
   - If a **big drop** is followed by:
     - A near-mirror **upward jump** → treat both as stock relocation or correction, not sales.
     - A long **flat line** → interpret almost no real sales during that period.
   - Only count **gradual stepwise decreases** as sales in your per-seller true-sales calculation.

2. **Keepa 30-day sold column as hard upper bound:**
   - For each seller:  
     **Under absolutely no circumstance** may your estimated 30-day sales **exceed** the Keepa “Sold (30 days)” number for that seller.
   - If Keepa shows “?” in “Sold (30 days)”, treat that as **0** for the **upper bound**.
   - If “?” appears only in Buy Box stats for stock, it just means they’re currently OOS for Buy Box purposes – it does **not automatically imply** 0 past sales.

3. **Cross-check vs “Bought in past month” (yellow line):**
   - Let **MonthlySold_market** = value from the **yellow line** “Bought in past month” on the main price/volume graph.
   - Let **SumTrueSales_graph** = sum of your per-seller true 30-day sales from the stock graphs (only those with visible stock history).
   - If there are sellers appearing **only** in Buy Box stats (no stock graphs, “?” stock now):
     - For cross-check **only**, you may approximate their 30-day sales as:  
       approx_sales = BuyBox% × MonthlySold_market.
     - **Never** use this approximation in the depletion forecast itself – only for validation.
   - Check:  
     SumTrueSales_graph + SumApprox_missing_sellers should be **close to** MonthlySold_market (±10 units).  
   - If not, **retrace** the graphs to look for misread big drops or miscounted steps. Do **not** force-fit the numbers; explain any persistent gap.

4. **OOS & “?” handling:**
   - If a seller is OOS now and only appears in **Buy Box stats with “?” stock**, **exclude them from the forward forecast**, but include them in the **back-test** of the last month where relevant.
   - Mark any seller or datum you cannot clearly interpret as:  
     UNVERIFIABLE – [brief reason].

5. **Seller behaviour classification (carry over from prior Phase 2):**
   - Re-assess **each in-stock seller** as:
     - **Aggressive BuyBox Chaser** – frequently matches/undercuts Buy Box in ≤1 day.
     - **Reactive BuyBox Chaser** – eventually converges to Buy Box, but with lag/infrequent steps.
     - **Premium (Slight / Moderate / High)** – structurally above Buy Box; rarely chases.

For each of N ≥ 10,000 iterations, simulate over 56 days:

∼NB(k,p).

Allocate demand via multinomial: alloc ~ Multinomial(D_t, p_t).

Clamp by stock: sales_i = min(alloc_i, stock_i).

stock_i ← stock_i − sales_i.

If stock_i hits 0, seller i is removed from future α’s.

Restocks: you defined a hazard λ_i = exp(−stock_i / θ) with θ in [24, 30]. Without a clear rule for:

when a hazard is evaluated (only when stock=0 or always?), and

how many units are restocked when it fires,
modelling restocks would be pure guesswork. I would leave λ_i as diagnostic only and assume no restocks to stay within your “no guessing” rule.

Daily & weekly sales for each seller (especially you).

Day/week of sell-out for each seller.

Repeat with N=5,000 and N=10,000; ensure key outputs (mean weekly sales, sell-out probabilities) move by <5%.

3.2 Discrepancies & conservatism

Even without exact numbers:

Monte-Carlo:

Captures demand variance and random order of stock-outs.

Will show fat tails: rare paths where you sell out very fast or very slowly.

Typically more conservative in terms of “probability of full sell-out” because some trajectories are unlucky.

Deterministic realistic:

Is a smoothed central path with no randomness.

Can underestimate spill-over when some sellers go OOS mid-week (we saw this in Week 1 and especially in Week 3).

Thus, it can be slightly conservative on your sales if you don’t reallocate leftover demand to the remaining sellers.

Overall, once Monte-Carlo is actually run:

I’d expect total units sold for you in Monte-Carlo mean to be within ~10–15% of the deterministic realistic forecast.

Any differences >15% would mostly come from:

Stochastic clustering of high-demand days.

Random sequences where big FBA rivals go OOS earlier or later than the deterministic week-boundaries.

3.3 Sensitivity (conceptual)

Again, without code we talk in directions:

Market volume ±20%

If weekly market volume drops to 0.8×93 ≈ 74/week:

Both Monte-Carlo and deterministic forecasts shift your sell-out roughly 1 week later (Week 5–6).

Total units over 8 weeks could fall into the 45–60 band.

If weekly market volume rises to 1.2×93 ≈ 112/week:

Sell-out moves 1 week earlier (Week 3–4).

The risk becomes stock-out too early rather than too late.

Your price ±£0.50

£11.00:

Price_score improves: 10.70/11.00 ≈ 0.973 vs 0.93 now.

Your BB share increases, especially vs other FBA.

You very likely sell out by Week 3–4 in both models.

Profit per unit shrinks (approx breakeven 11.14 → you may be just at or slightly below true breakeven depending on fees). You’re trading profit for speed.

£12.00:

Price_score worsens: 10.70/12.00 ≈ 0.892.

BB share shifts toward cheaper FBA/FBM; you become the premium FBA.

Deterministic & Monte-Carlo will both push your sell-out toward Week 5–7, with a non-trivial probability of not selling out within 8 weeks.

Profit per unit increases, but only if the units actually move.

GO, but as a learning SKU, not a profit engine.

Realistic: ~10–20 units/week, sell-out ~Week 4–5.

Worst: ~7–12 units/week, partial sell-through (~45–55 units) by Week 8.

I’ve put a full Monte Carlo simulator into a canvas file called “Keepa Monte Carlo Depletion Simulator” (Python). You can open it in the side panel and edit it directly.

Below are the steps to use it and how everything fits together, so you can run the simulations yourself.

1. What the script already does

Inside the canvas you’ll see a Python file that:

Defines your sellers

@dataclass
class Seller:
    name: str
    price: float          # inc VAT
    reviews: int
    stock: int            # starting stock
    is_fba: bool
    hist_weekly_rate: float
    is_me: bool = False   # mark your listing


And a SELLERS = [...] list with example entries including one named "ME" (your listing).

Sets market + model parameters

At the top of the file (CONFIG section):

MONTHLY_SOLD_MARKET – from Keepa “Bought in past month” (yellow line).

WEEKLY_MARKET_VOLUME = MONTHLY_SOLD_MARKET / 4.3.

NEG_BINOMIAL_K – dispersion parameter (1.2 by default).

NUM_WEEKS = 8, NUM_ITERATIONS = 10_000, etc.

Implements the Monte Carlo

Converts weekly volume into daily mean and builds Negative Binomial params.

For each iteration and each day:

Filters in-stock sellers.

Computes Dirichlet α_i based on:

FBA vs FBM

price competitiveness

review count

relative stock

Samples Buy Box shares from a Dirichlet.

Samples daily demand from a Negative Binomial.

Allocates demand with a Multinomial, clamps to stock, updates stocks.

Records:

daily sales per seller

daily BB share per seller

day each seller goes OOS.

Summarises results

summarise_results(...) prints:

For every seller:

Weekly mean sales and 95% CI for weeks 1–8.

Probability of being sold out by week 1,2,…,8.

For your listing (is_me=True):

Weekly Buy Box share mean + 95% CI.

Distribution of total units sold in 8 weeks.

Revenue & profit (using your £0.30 net profit/unit).

2. What you need to edit before running

Open the canvas file and update these parts:

Market volume

MONTHLY_SOLD_MARKET = 400.0  # <- replace with latest yellow-line value
WEEKLY_MARKET_VOLUME = MONTHLY_SOLD_MARKET / 4.3


Your listing parameters

Find the "ME" seller in SELLERS and edit:

Seller("ME",
       price=11.50,     # your chosen price (inc VAT)
       reviews=0,
       stock=60,        # number of units you will send in
       is_fba=True,
       hist_weekly_rate=0.0,
       is_me=True)


Other sellers

For each competitor, plug in from the offers table:

price = their new price inc VAT.

reviews = their lifetime review count.

stock = their current FBA/FBM stock (from Keepa stock column).

is_fba = True if Prime/FBA, False if FBM.

hist_weekly_rate = their true 30-day sales / 4.3 from your Phase-1 work.

Add/remove Seller(...) rows so the list matches the current competition.

Iterations

If you want a quicker test run:

NUM_ITERATIONS = 5000


Then switch back to 10_000 once you’re happy.

3. How to run the script (step-by-step)

On your PC (assuming Python 3.10+ installed):

Download the file from canvas if needed, save as
keepa_monte_carlo_depletion_simulator.py.

Open a terminal / PowerShell in that folder.

(Optional but recommended) Create a venv:

python -m venv .venv
.\.venv\Scripts\activate  # Windows
# or source .venv/bin/activate on WSL/Linux/macOS


Install numpy:

pip install numpy


Run the simulation:

python keepa_monte_carlo_depletion_simulator.py


Read the output:

It first prints a CONFIG SUMMARY (so you can visually confirm you input the right stocks/prices).

Then:

Weekly sales per seller with 95% CI.

Sell-out probabilities per seller.

For you: weekly BB share, plus 8-week units, revenue and profit with 95% CI.

If you want CSV outputs, I can extend the script with a small function that writes sales_by_week and bb_by_week to files; right now it prints to console only.

4. How this maps to your spec

Negative Binomial demand
Implemented via numpy.random.negative_binomial using parameters derived from:

mean = WEEKLY_MARKET_VOLUME / 7

dispersion k = NEG_BINOMIAL_K.

Dirichlet BB shares
Implemented in build_alpha_vector() with:

alpha = 0.65 * FBA_score \
      + 0.225 * price_score \
      + 0.05 * review_score \
      + 0.085 * stock_score


where:

FBA_score = 1.0 (FBA) or 0.3 (FBM)

price_score = clip(min_price / seller_price, 0.80, 1.10)

review_score = reviews / max_reviews

stock_score = stock / total_stock.

Sell-out logic & renormalisation
Once a seller’s stock hits 0, they simply drop out of the in_play mask and stop receiving demand allocations.

Revenue/profit
Uses your baseline net profit per unit (£0.30). You can change this in summarise_results.

5. If you want to extend this yourself

Here are the natural places to plug in more logic:

Best / Realistic / Worst scenarios

Duplicate SELLERS into three variants (e.g. SELLERS_BEST, SELLERS_REAL, SELLERS_WORST) with:

Best: competitors +5% price or -20% stock.

Worst: add a new FBM Seller with low price and large stock.

Call simulate_paths three times and label outputs.

Deterministic model

Use the same Seller class.

Implement the weekly formula:

Sales_week_i = hist_weekly_rate_i * w_factor + BuyBoxShare_i_week * WEEKLY_MARKET_VOLUME


Use the same FBA/Price/Reviews/Stock scores to get deterministic BuyBoxShare_i_week.

Short answer first:

Yes, the script in canvas really does compute the numeric 95% intervals I was talking about — for weekly sales, Buy Box share, total units, revenue and profit — if you run it on your machine.

When I previously said “I can’t honestly give numeric 95% intervals without actually running Monte Carlo”, I meant: inside that reply I was not actually executing any simulation, so giving hard numbers would have been made-up.

Earlier “Monte Carlo” descriptions were method descriptions, not code that was actually run with your real parameters.

Now I’ll unpack the limitation clearly, step by step, no analogies.

1. What the script actually outputs

If you run the script as it is (after you plug in correct SELLERS + MONTHLY_SOLD_MARKET):

It simulates NUM_ITERATIONS independent 8-week futures.

It records, for each run, how many units each seller sold each day.

Then, in summarise_results() it does this:

sales_by_week = sales_paths.reshape(iters, sellers, weeks, 7).sum(axis=3)
p2_5 = np.percentile(weekly,  2.5, axis=0)
p97_5 = np.percentile(weekly, 97.5, axis=0)


That is a real Monte Carlo interval:

p2_5[w] = the 2.5th percentile of simulated Week-w sales.

p97_5[w] = the 97.5th percentile of simulated Week-w sales.

Same idea for:

Buy Box share per week (percentiles of bb_share_paths).

Total 8-week units, then revenue and profit percentiles.

So yes, the script produces the numeric 95% intervals I described, as long as you actually execute it.

2. What I can and cannot do inside ChatGPT
What I can do

I can write the full algorithm:

distributions,

loops,

calculations,

percentile logic.

I can show formulas and explain how to compute things.

I can even run small pieces of code in my own sandbox if we hard-code parameters.

What I cannot honestly claim unless I run the code

A Monte Carlo 95% interval is not just a formula; it depends on the actual random draws that happened in that simulation:

Which exact Negative Binomial numbers came out for each day.

Which exact Dirichlet vectors and resulting BB allocations came out each day.

How those flows interacted with the stock constraints.

If I do not execute the simulation with real draws, then:

I do not know the real sample 2.5th and 97.5th percentiles for your config.

I can only give theoretical approximations (e.g. mean ± 1.96·sd), which is not the same as the Monte Carlo output you asked for.

So in that earlier answer, I was saying:

“I am not actually running 10,000 random paths right now, so I cannot honestly print ‘Week 1 95% CI = [17, 31]’ and pretend it comes from a real Monte Carlo run.”

If I gave any concrete CI numbers in that context, treat them as illustrative, not real simulation output — that would be my mistake, and that’s exactly why I moved to giving you actual executable code.

3. What I did for the “first Monte Carlo simulation”

In the earlier phase, I did this:

Designed the structure of the Monte Carlo:

Negative Binomial for demand.

Dirichlet for BB share.

Daily loop with stock clamping.

Described qualitatively what the results would look like:

Which sellers tend to sell out first.

How BB share might move.

I did not:

Run your exact numbers through an actual random simulation and collect the raw paths.

Compute sample percentiles from those paths.

So the “first Monte Carlo” was:

A specification and logical reasoning about behaviour.

Not a concrete execution with stored outputs.

The script you now have is the first fully wired implementation that, when run, will output real numeric 95% intervals.

4. Where the exact limitation sits – with plain examples

I’ll use direct statements, no comparisons.

Example 1 – Mean vs Monte Carlo interval

If you tell me:

Monthly sold = 400

So weekly mean ≈ 400 / 4.3 ≈ 93

So daily mean ≈ 93 / 7 ≈ 13.3

I can compute:

Expected 8-week units for the whole market ≈ 8 × 93 ≈ 744.

But a Monte Carlo 95% interval for 8-week market units depends on:

The actual 10,000 streams of Negative Binomial numbers.

Each stream is a concrete list of 56 integers (one per day).

Without running:

I can write the probability law.

I cannot state “2.5% of runs ended below 700 and 97.5% below 790” because those numbers come from counting outcomes in actual runs.

Example 2 – Your listing’s units

Your listing’s 8-week units in simulation depend on:

How often it gets the Buy Box on each day.

On which days competitors go to stock = 0 in each run.

On the exact draws of the multinomial allocations.

Those are path-dependent. The 95% interval for your units is:

Take all 10,000 simulated totals.

Sort them.

Pick values at positions 250 and 9750 (2.5th and 97.5th percent).

No simulation → no sorted list → no honest percentile.

5. Why the script fixes this problem for you

The script:

Takes your inputs (stocks, prices, reviews).

Generates actual random paths in Python (numpy).

Stores all per-seller sales.

Uses np.percentile to compute the central 95% of outcomes.

So when you run it, the 95% intervals are:

Real sample statistics for your configuration, not invented numbers.

Reproducible (same RANDOM_SEED → same intervals).

If you want, I can also add:

Saving the raw per-iteration totals to CSV.

A convergence check: run 5,000 vs 10,000 iterations inside the same script and print how much the mean and CI endpoints move.

3.1. Seller-level depletion comparison

Monte Carlo values are extracted from your sell-out probabilities.

Naive depletion weeks: "if they kept the Phase-1 weekly rate forever,
                         ignoring randomness and BB shifts."
Monte Carlo median week: first week where cumulative P(sell-out) ≥ 50%.
Monte Carlo 90% window: approximate week where cumulative P crosses 5% and 95%.

1.2. New run_scenario helper

At the bottom, before main(), there’s now:

def run_scenario(
    label: str,
    monthly_sold_market: float,
    sellers: List[Seller],
    num_weeks: int,
    num_iterations: int,
    k: float,
    seed: int,
) -> Dict[str, np.ndarray]:
    """Run a single scenario (initial vs current, etc.) and print results.

    This lets you plug in the first-screenshot config as one scenario and
    the latest-screenshot config as another, so you can directly compare
    forecasts vs actuals.
    """
    weekly_market_volume = monthly_sold_market / 4.3
    print("\n\nSCENARIO:", label)
    print("CONFIG SUMMARY")
    print("--------------")
    print(f"MonthlySold_market     = {monthly_sold_market:.1f}")
    print(f"WeeklyMarketVolume     = {weekly_market_volume:.2f}")
    print(f"NegBinomial k          = {k:.2f}")
    print(f"Num weeks              = {num_weeks}")
    print(f"Num iterations         = {num_iterations}")
    print("\nSellers:")
    for s in sellers:
        flag = " (YOU)" if s.is_me else ""
        print(
            f"  - {s.name}{flag}: price={s.price}, reviews={s.reviews}, stock={s.stock}, "
            f"is_fba={s.is_fba}, hist_weekly_rate={s.hist_weekly_rate:.2f}"
        )

    raw = simulate_paths(
        sellers=sellers,
        weekly_market_volume=weekly_market_volume,
        k=k,
        num_weeks=num_weeks,
        num_iterations=num_iterations,
        seed=seed,
    )

    summarise_results(sellers, raw, num_weeks)
    return raw


So run_scenario(...):

Calculates weekly_market_volume = monthly_sold / 4.3.

Prints config summary + sellers list.

Runs simulate_paths(...).

Calls summarise_results(...) → same style of output you already saw.

Returns the raw arrays if you ever want to do extra comparison in code later.

1.3. main() now runs one or two scenarios

The old main() (which only used MONTHLY_SOLD_MARKET + SELLERS) is replaced with:

def main() -> None:
    # Optional initial snapshot scenario for back-testing against later actuals.
    if RUN_INITIAL_SCENARIO and INITIAL_SELLERS:
        run_scenario(
            label="INITIAL SNAPSHOT (first Keepa screenshots)",
            monthly_sold_market=INITIAL_MONTHLY_SOLD_MARKET,
            sellers=INITIAL_SELLERS,
            num_weeks=NUM_WEEKS,
            num_iterations=NUM_ITERATIONS,
            k=NEG_BINOMIAL_K,
            seed=RANDOM_SEED,
        )

    # Current scenario using the latest Keepa screenshots.
    run_scenario(
        label="CURRENT SNAPSHOT (latest Keepa screenshots)",
        monthly_sold_market=MONTHLY_SOLD_MARKET,
        sellers=SELLERS,
        num_weeks=NUM_WEEKS,
        num_iterations=NUM_ITERATIONS,
        k=NEG_BINOMIAL_K,
        seed=RANDOM_SEED,
    )


So when you run:

python keepa_monte_carlo_depletion_simulator.py


you’ll get:

If RUN_INITIAL_SCENARIO = True and INITIAL_SELLERS not empty:
SCENARIO: INITIAL SNAPSHOT (first Keepa screenshots) with:

Weekly sales (mean, 95% CI) per seller.

Sell-out probabilities per week.

Your BB share / revenue / profit if you’re present in that snapshot.

Then always:
SCENARIO: CURRENT SNAPSHOT (latest Keepa screenshots) – the output you already saw, but now clearly labelled.

This lets you:

Treat the initial scenario as “what we would have forecasted at time T0”.

Treat the current scenario + actual 30-day sales from second screenshots as reality.

Compare how close the initial Monte Carlo structure was to what actually unfolded.

Was our choice of k=1.2 and Dirichlet α construction too volatile or too conservative?

That is exactly the “how accurate and deterministic is this approach?” check you were aiming for.

def run_scenario(
    label: str,
    monthly_sold_market: float,
    sellers: List[Seller],
    num_weeks: int,
    num_iterations: int,
    k: float,
    seed: int,
) -> Dict[str, np.ndarray]:
    """Run a single scenario (initial vs current, etc.) and print results.

    This lets you plug in the first-screenshot config as one scenario and
    the latest-screenshot config as another, so you can directly compare
    forecasts vs actuals.
    """

    weekly_market_volume = monthly_sold_market / 4.3

    print(f"\n\nSCENARIO: {label}")
    print("CONFIG SUMMARY")
    print("--------------")
    print(f"MonthlySold_market     = {monthly_sold_market:.1f}")
    print(f"WeeklyMarketVolume     = {weekly_market_volume:.2f}")
    print(f"NegBinomial k          = {k:.2f}")
    print(f"Num weeks              = {num_weeks}")
    print(f"Num iterations         = {num_iterations}")
    print("\nSellers:")
    for s in sellers:
        flag = " (YOU)" if s.is_me else ""
        print(
            f"  - {s.name}{flag}: price={s.price}, reviews={s.reviews}, stock={s.stock}, "
            f"is_fba={s.is_fba}, hist_weekly_rate={s.hist_weekly_rate:.2f}"
        )

    raw = simulate_paths(
        sellers=sellers,
        weekly_market_volume=weekly_market_volume,
        k=k,
        num_weeks=num_weeks,
        num_iterations=num_iterations,
        seed=seed,
    )

    summarise_results(sellers, raw, num_weeks)
    return raw


Everything else (Negative Binomial, Dirichlet, initial vs current scenarios, etc.) is unchanged in behaviour.

Negative Binomial parameters:

3. What exactly the current approach does (step-by-step, with numbers)

I’ll use the latest snapshot you actually ran, since you pasted the output.

3.1 Inputs used

From your data:

Market:

MonthlySold_market = 400 (yellow line).

WeeklyMarketVolume = 400 / 4.3 ≈ 93.02 units.

Mean daily demand μ ≈ 93.02 / 7 ≈ 13.29 units.

Sellers:

Seller	Price	Reviews	Stock	FBA?	Hist weekly rate
YourEverydayStuff	10.75	35243	40	FBM	32.8
E-com height	11.15	4764	46	FBA	18.8
Northmax	11.25	26	67	FBA	18.1
REVON LTD	10.70	2996	3	FBM	15.1
Lioncrest Retail	10.99	47	13	FBA	8.1
ME	11.50	0	60	FBA	0.0

Note: hist_weekly_rate is only used in the sense that it reflects how strong they were historically; the Dirichlet α is built mostly from price, FBA, reviews, and stock.

3.2 Daily demand generation

Every day, for each of 10,000 runs:

Draw a random total demand D_t from Negative Binomial with mean ≈ 13.29 and dispersion k = 1.2.
So some days D_t might be 5, some days 20, some days 0, etc. Over many days, the average stays close to 13.29.

3.3 Buy Box weights per seller

For the sellers that still have stock that day, we compute:

FBA_score:

FBA → 1.0

FBM → 0.3

Price_score:

min_price / seller_price, clipped to [0.80, 1.10].

In your case min_price = REVON LTD at 10.70.

Example:

For ME at 11.50:

ratio = 10.70 / 11.50 ≈ 0.93.

clipped stays 0.93.

For YourEverydayStuff at 10.75:

ratio ≈ 10.70 / 10.75 ≈ 0.996 → clipped ≈ 0.996.

Review_score:

seller_reviews / max_reviews.

max_reviews = 35243 (YourEverydayStuff).

Example:

YourEverydayStuff: 35243 / 35243 = 1.0.

E-com height: 4764 / 35243 ≈ 0.135.

Stock_score:

stock / total stock among in-stock sellers that day.

Then:

alpha_i = 0.65 * FBA_score_i + 0.225 * Price_score_i + 0.05 * Review_score_i + 0.085 * Stock_score_i.

This α-vector captures “power” for each seller that day. Higher α means the seller more often gets a high Buy Box share, but there is still randomness.

Then we sample:

BB_share_vec_t ~ Dirichlet(alpha_1, ..., alpha_n).

This gives a random Buy Box share for each seller that day, but on average, those shares respect the FBA advantage, price advantage, reviews and stock.

3.4 Demand allocation and stock updates

For that day:

Use D_t (total demand) and BB_share_vec_t to allocate the buyers:

Example: if D_t = 15 and ME’s share that day is 0.25, expected buyers allocated to ME ≈ 3–4, but actual drawn by multinomial might be 2, 3, 4, etc.

For each seller:

sales_today = min(allocated_demand, current_stock).

Update stock:

stock = stock - sales_today.

If stock reaches zero for a seller:

Record the day of stock-out.

Remove them from the next days’ seller list, so their α is no longer used, and remaining sellers get more Buy Box share.

We repeat this for 56 days (8 weeks), and for 10,000 iterations of the whole 56-day path.

3.5 Aggregation into weekly statistics

After all runs:

Sum the daily sales into weeks:

Week 1 = days 1–7

Week 2 = days 8–14

… up to Week 8.

For each seller and week:

Mean weekly sales.

2.5% percentile and 97.5% percentile (this forms the 95% interval).

For each seller:

Probability that stockout_day ≤ 7, ≤ 14, ≤ 21, …, ≤ 56.

This gives P(sell-out by end of week w).

For your listing:

Weekly Buy Box share mean and 95% interval.

Total units sold distribution, revenue, and profit distribution.

By 8 weeks, you basically always sell all 60 units.

Weekly sales are volatile.

That range is wide: in some paths, day-to-day randomness gives you only a handful of orders in week 1; in other paths you get a large share and move 40–50 units in week 1 alone.

## 2. Modelling choices for the 7-week simulation

**Market volume**

* Price history says: **“Bought in past month: 300+”** → I anchor at **300 units/month**.
* Weekly market volume used in the formula:
  **MarketVolume ≈ 300 / 4.3 ≈ 69.8 units/week.**

**Per-seller historical weekly rate**

* From Phase 1 “true 30-day sales” (not the raw Keepa 30-day column):

| Seller                | True 30-day sales (Phase 1) |
| --------------------- | --------------------------- |
| REVON LTD             | 90                          |
| YourEverydayStuff     | 90                          |
| Northmax              | 76                          |
| The Online Stores FBA | 2                           |
| E-com height          | 16                          |
| Elteuara              | 13                          |
| Hexses Outlet FBA     | 9                           |
| The Online Stores FBM | 3                           |
| Hexses Outlet FBM     | 1                           |
| Shakti Housewares     | 1                           |
| YOU (new FBA)         | 0                           |

* HistWeeklyRate = true_30d / 4.3.

**Buy Box share formula per week**

For all **in-stock** sellers that week:

* Compute four normalized components:

  * **FBA vs FBM (65%)**

    * Weight 1.0 if FBA, 0.3 if FBM.
    * Normalized across active sellers → gives 65% of BB share.
  * **Price competitiveness (22.5%)**

    * PriceScore = (min_price / seller_price).
    * Normalized → 22.5% of BB share.
  * **Reviews (5%)**

    * Score ≈ √(review_count) to avoid mega-store domination.
    * Sellers without stats get a small baseline score.
    * Normalized → 5% of BB share.
  * **Stock (8.5%)**

    * Proportional to current stock → 8.5% of BB share.
* Sum of components = **BuyBoxShare_seller** (sums to 1.0 across sellers with stock>0).

**Weekly sales per seller**

For week t:

[
\text{Sales}_\text{week} = (\text{HistWeeklyRate} \times w) + (\text{BuyBoxShare} \times \text{MarketVolume})
]

* **w = 0.3** for Weeks 1–2; **w = 0.2** for Weeks 3–7.
* If calculated sales > current stock → clamp to stock and mark OOS.

This construction naturally pushes total weekly sales slightly above 69.8 when a lot of stock is available, and below when many sellers run out. I **did not force-fit** totals back to 69.8; see cross-check later.

## 4. Best-case scenario

**Changes vs Realistic**

* Every *existing* rival:

  * Raises price by **5%**.
  * Has **20% less starting stock** (e.g. 21 → 17, 123 → 98, etc.).
* Your listing remains **60 FBA @ £11.50**.

**Your series (Best-case)**

* Weekly stock trajectory (approx):

  * W1: 60 → **51.7** (≈ 8.3 sold)
  * W2: 51.7 → **35.8** (≈ 16.0 sold)
  * W3: 35.8 → **11.4** (≈ 24.4 sold)
  * W4: 11.4 → **0** (≈ 11.4 sold)
  * W5–7: 0 (OOS)

* So:

  * **Sell-out week:** **4** (slightly more front-loaded than Realistic).
  * **Total sold:** 60 units (same).
  * **Revenue:** ~£690; **gross profit** ~£24 (same).

**Market weekly totals (Best)**

* ≈ **73.8, 68.7, 74.4, 53.9, 70.7, 70.7, 47.5**
* Notice how after Week 4 the market is materially under-served because many rivals are OOS and demand is not fully satisfied. That’s exactly why your Week-1–3 share jumps.

## 5. Worst-case scenario

**Changes vs Realistic**

* All existing sellers unchanged.
* A **new Aggressive FBM rival** enters:

  * Undercuts everyone by **≈3% below the current cheapest price**.
  * Has strong prior history in the model (**120 sales / 30 days**) and substantial stock (~25% of total combined stock).
  * FBM but highly price-competitive, so it eats a lot of the price-weighted share.

**Your series (Worst-case)**

* Weekly stock trajectory:

  * W1: 60 → **52.4** (≈ 7.6 sold)
  * W2: 52.4 → **38.1** (≈ 14.3 sold)
  * W3: 38.1 → **17.1** (≈ 21.1 sold)
  * W4: 17.1 → **0** (≈ 17.1 sold)
  * W5–7: 0 (OOS)

* Even in this stacked scenario:

  * **Sell-out week:** still **4** (sales are slower in W1–2 but catch up later).
  * **Total sold:** 60 units.
  * **Revenue & profit:** again ~£690 revenue / ~£24 gross.

**Market weekly totals (Worst)**

* ≈ **88.7, 87.7, 80.5, 74.2, 76.3, 76.3, 76.3**.

These exceed the 69.8/week cross-check in the first three weeks because we’ve explicitly added an extra high-volume competitor *on top of* the existing 300-per-month baseline. I’m not trying to reconcile that back down artificially; the point of this scenario is stress-testing your share if additional volume is dumped into the market.

## 6. Summary comparison – your listing only

Rounded, 7-week horizon:

| Scenario  | W1 sales | W2 sales | W3 sales | W4 sales | Week sold out | Units sold | Revenue (£11.50) | Approx gross profit* |
| --------- | -------- | -------- | -------- | -------- | ------------- | ---------- | ---------------- | -------------------- |
| Best      | ~8       | ~16      | ~24      | ~11      | 4             | 60         | ~£690            | ~£24                 |
| Realistic | ~8       | ~16      | ~24      | ~12      | 4             | 60         | ~£690            | ~£24                 |
| Worst     | ~8       | ~14      | ~21      | ~17      | 4             | 60         | ~£690            | ~£24                 |

*Using breakeven ≈ £11.10 from your calculator; if your true per-unit breakeven changes, scale the profit accordingly.

Key takeaway: **timing shifts a bit, but in all three simulations you clear 60 units comfortably within 4 weeks**, even at £11.50.

---

## 7. Recommendations (≤8 bullets)

1. **Enter the listing is acceptable**: with 300+ monthly units and your relatively small 60-unit batch, the main risk is not getting *any* Buy Box exposure. The model suggests you *do* get a slice even at £11.50, mainly because you’re FBA and many rivals have limited FBA stock.

2. **Price positioning:** £11.50 is at the **top end** of the current FBA range (vs ~£10.74–11.10). If you want a more conservative entry, consider a **launch band of £11.29–£11.49**, then creep up only if Buy Box holds and sell-through is strong.

3. **Watch REVON + YourEverydayStuff closely:** they are the **true aggressors** (FBM but heavy BB share). If either drops significantly below ~£10.70, expect pressure on the FBA cluster and potential Buy Box compression.

4. **FBA cluster is shallow:** current FBA sellers with meaningful stock (Northmax, E-com height, Elteuara, Hexses FBA, The Online Stores FBA) collectively hold **small quantities**. This is good for you – FBA stock churns quickly, so you don’t need a huge share to sell 60 units.

5. **Big FBM inventories are “parked”:** The Online Stores FBM, Hexses FBM, and Shakti have large stocks but low BB influence. They mainly matter in worst-case price-war scenarios or if Amazon shifts more Buy Box to non-Prime.

6. **Monitor real vs modelled weekly totals:** if you see the real market selling clearly <70/week for several weeks, **downgrade** the optimistic side of this forecast and assume your best/worst curves are shifted right by ~1 week.

7. **Exit rule:** if Buy Box collapses below **~£10.70** and stays there for more than a week with FBA competition still heavy, you’re too far above breakeven to play that game. Plan in advance whether you’d (a) accept a small loss at a lower price, or (b) dump via other channels.

8. **Re-run with your exact fee inputs:** once you lock in the final FBA + referral + prep numbers, re-plug the breakeven; if breakeven turns out higher than 11.10, the tiny £24 gross becomes even thinner and you may prefer a **faster liquidation price** with minimal loss instead of chasing profit.

Thought for 5m 36s

Here is the clean comparison table that pulls everything together:

Phase 1 (first Keepa batch) → your initial “true 30-day” sales and deterministic depletion forecast.

Phase 1 Monte Carlo → 8-week stochastic depletion based on the first snapshot.

Phase 2 / “actual” → second Keepa batch (higher “Bought in past month” ≈ 400) and Monte Carlo on the current snapshot.

I am not inventing any weekly “actual” path, because the second Keepa batch only gives you a new 30-day rate and a new starting stock, not a week-by-week history. So “actual” here means: updated true 30-day sales and current stocks from the second screenshots, plus the Monte Carlo output you just ran on them.

Integrated depletion comparison – all sellers

“True 30d sales – Phase 1” = your Phase-1 manual “true sales” over last 30 days from the first batch.

“True 30d sales – Phase 2” = derived from hist_weekly_rate × 4.3 in the current snapshot (if the seller appears there).

“Det. sell-out week (Phase 1)” = from the 7-week deterministic table we built before.

“MC 8w mean units (Phase 1)” = sum of weekly Monte Carlo means from the INITIAL SNAPSHOT block.

“MC P(sell-out ≤ W4)” = probability (from the script) that the seller is out of stock by end of week 4.

“MC 8w mean units (Phase 2)” & “P(sell-out ≤ W4, Phase 2)” = same idea but from the CURRENT SNAPSHOT block. Where the seller doesn’t exist in Phase 2, it’s marked as UNVERIFIABLE – not present in 2nd snapshot.

All values rounded sensibly (1 decimal for volumes, 1 decimal for probabilities when needed).

| Seller                    | Type | Behaviour (Phase 1)                          | Initial stock (P1 / P2) | True 30d sales – Phase 1 | True 30d sales – Phase 2* | Det. sell-out week (Phase 1 model) | MC 8w mean units (Phase 1) | MC P(sell-out ≤ W4, Phase 1) | MC 8w mean units (Phase 2) | MC P(sell-out ≤ W4, Phase 2) | Notes                                                                                          |
|---------------------------|------|----------------------------------------------|-------------------------|---------------------------|-----------------------------|-------------------------------------|-----------------------------|-------------------------------|-----------------------------|-------------------------------|------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------|
| REVON LTD                 | FBM  | Aggressive BuyBox chaser                     | 21 / 3                 | 90                        | ≈64.9                       | Week 2 (21 → 0 by our table)       | ≈21.0 (all 21 sold)         | 78.0%                         | ≈3.0 (all 3 sold)           | 100.0%                        | High historical velocity; both models say small Phase-1 batch clears fast. Phase-2 snapshot shows they are still an active high-volume seller with a tiny visible stock at the current moment. |
| YourEverydayStuff         | FBM  | Aggressive undercutter / BuyBox chaser       | 53 / 40                | 90                        | ≈141.0                      | Week 4 (53 → 0)                     | ≈52.9 (almost all 53)       | 15.4%                         | ≈40.0 (all 40 sold)         | 98.7%                         | In Phase 1 deterministic we assumed they clear by W4; MC Phase 1 is more conservative (only 15% chance to empty by W4). *Phase-2 “True 30d sales” is computed from the hist_weekly_rate in the CURRENT SNAPSHOT output: Monthly ≈ hist_weekly_rate × 4.3. Where the seller does not appear in the second run, their Phase-2 sales are marked as UNVERIFIABLE – not present in 2nd snapshot.

How this helps you compare forecasts vs reality

With this table you can now see, per seller:

How aggressive your Phase-1 depletion assumptions were

Deterministic Phase 1 assumed all the small FBA sellers (E-com, Elteuara, Hexses FBA, The Online Stores FBA) and your own 60 units would clear extremely fast (Week 1–4).

For the very large FBM “parking lots” (Online Stores FBM, Hexses FBM, Shakti), it assumed they would not deplete over 7 weeks.

What the Monte Carlo (Phase 1) actually implied

For small-stock sellers (including you) it essentially agrees: all stock is gone within 8 weeks, but it quantifies probabilities of selling out by Week 4 instead of hard yes/no.

Example: you have 54.7% chance to be empty by end of Week 4 in Phase-1 MC, versus “guaranteed” in deterministic.

For big FBM buckets, the MC model pushes far more volume to them than their historical 30-day sales (because of the way stock and price enter the Dirichlet weights). That is exactly the kind of bias you wanted to expose.

What the second snapshot (“actual” 30d period) is telling us

Market volume increased from ≈301 to ≈400 in the yellow line.

Some Phase-1 players scaled up massively (E-com height 16 → ~81 30d sold; Northmax ~76 → ~78 but now holding 67 FBA units).

Several Phase-1 “extra” sellers disappear or become irrelevant (Elteuara, Hexses FBA, both “The Online Stores” listings, Hexses FBM, Shakti are not in your second scripted scenario at all).

A new FBA seller (Lioncrest) appears with decent 30d volume and a 13-unit batch.

How the recalibrated Monte Carlo reacts to the new reality

In the current snapshot, the MC run expects all six visible sellers (YourEverydayStuff, E-com height, Northmax, REVON, Lioncrest, you) to sell 100% of their available stock within 8 weeks, with very high probabilities of selling out by Week 4 for all of them.

For you specifically, Phase-2 MC is much more bullish than Phase-1 MC on timing (99.2% vs 54.7% sell-out by Week 4), because:

Overall market volume is higher (400 vs ~300 per month), and

The competitive set is now dominated by a few high-volume FBA/FBM listings with relatively limited total stock, so once they start to exhaust, your FBA slot gets more demand.

Where the original deterministic model was too confident / mis-calibrated

From this table you can see the key biases:

For you and other small FBA lots in Phase 1, the deterministic model’s “hard” Week-4 sell-out assumption is more aggressive than the Monte Carlo probabilities (54.7% is not “guaranteed”).

For the big FBM tanks, deterministic was arguably too conservative about how much they might sell in 8 weeks (it anchored to historical 30-day sales). Monte Carlo, by contrast, may be too optimistic for those players because it gives a lot of weight to stock size and price even when true 30d sales are tiny (1–3 units).

What this means for depletion-rate forecasting going forward

You now have two calibration levers:

Use the Phase-2 true 30d sales per seller (400-unit month) as a new baseline for hist_weekly_rate instead of Phase-1 values.

Tweak the Dirichlet weights (especially the Stock component) if you think MC is still over-feeding the giant FBM buckets compared to reality.

You can also set rules like:

“If Phase-1 and Phase-2 true 30-day sales for a seller differ by more than X%, dampen the historical component in the deterministic formula”

“Cap the MC implied 8-week volume for any seller at some multiplier of their Phase-2 30-day true sales unless we have evidence of a stock-dump event.”

## 2. Modelling choices for the 7-week simulation

**Market volume**

* Price history says: **“Bought in past month: 300+”** → I anchor at **300 units/month**.
* Weekly market volume used in the formula:
  **MarketVolume ≈ 300 / 4.3 ≈ 69.8 units/week.**

**Per-seller historical weekly rate**

* From Phase 1 “true 30-day sales” (not the raw Keepa 30-day column):

| Seller                | True 30-day sales (Phase 1) |
| --------------------- | --------------------------- |
| REVON LTD             | 90                          |
| YourEverydayStuff     | 90                          |
| Northmax              | 76                          |
| The Online Stores FBA | 2                           |
| E-com height          | 16                          |
| Elteuara              | 13                          |
| Hexses Outlet FBA     | 9                           |
| The Online Stores FBM | 3                           |
| Hexses Outlet FBM     | 1                           |
| Shakti Housewares     | 1                           |
| YOU (new FBA)         | 0                           |

* HistWeeklyRate = true_30d / 4.3.

**Buy Box share formula per week**

For all **in-stock** sellers that week:

* Compute four normalized components:

  * **FBA vs FBM (65%)**

    * Weight 1.0 if FBA, 0.3 if FBM.
    * Normalized across active sellers → gives 65% of BB share.
  * **Price competitiveness (22.5%)**

    * PriceScore = (min_price / seller_price).
    * Normalized → 22.5% of BB share.
  * **Reviews (5%)**

    * Score ≈ √(review_count) to avoid mega-store domination.
    * Sellers without stats get a small baseline score.
    * Normalized → 5% of BB share.
  * **Stock (8.5%)**

    * Proportional to current stock → 8.5% of BB share.
* Sum of components = **BuyBoxShare_seller** (sums to 1.0 across sellers with stock>0).

**Weekly sales per seller**

For week t:

[
\text{Sales}_\text{week} = (\text{HistWeeklyRate} \times w) + (\text{BuyBoxShare} \times \text{MarketVolume})
]

* **w = 0.3** for Weeks 1–2; **w = 0.2** for Weeks 3–7.
* If calculated sales > current stock → clamp to stock and mark OOS.

This construction naturally pushes total weekly sales slightly above 69.8 when a lot of stock is available, and below when many sellers run out. I **did not force-fit** totals back to 69.8; see cross-check later.

  * W2: 52.4 → **38.1** (≈ 14.3 sold)

## 7. Recommendations (≤8 bullets)

1. **Enter the listing is acceptable**: with 300+ monthly units and your relatively small 60-unit batch, the main risk is not getting *any* Buy Box exposure. The model suggests you *do* get a slice even at £11.50, mainly because you’re FBA and many rivals have limited FBA stock.

2. **Price positioning:** £11.50 is at the **top end** of the current FBA range (vs ~£10.74–11.10). If you want a more conservative entry, consider a **launch band of £11.29–£11.49**, then creep up only if Buy Box holds and sell-through is strong.

3. **Watch REVON + YourEverydayStuff closely:** they are the **true aggressors** (FBM but heavy BB share). If either drops significantly below ~£10.70, expect pressure on the FBA cluster and potential Buy Box compression.

4. **FBA cluster is shallow:** current FBA sellers with meaningful stock (Northmax, E-com height, Elteuara, Hexses FBA, The Online Stores FBA) collectively hold **small quantities**. This is good for you – FBA stock churns quickly, so you don’t need a huge share to sell 60 units.

5. **Big FBM inventories are “parked”:** The Online Stores FBM, Hexses FBM, and Shakti have large stocks but low BB influence. They mainly matter in worst-case price-war scenarios or if Amazon shifts more Buy Box to non-Prime.

6. **Monitor real vs modelled weekly totals:** if you see the real market selling clearly <70/week for several weeks, **downgrade** the optimistic side of this forecast and assume your best/worst curves are shifted right by ~1 week.

7. **Exit rule:** if Buy Box collapses below **~£10.70** and stays there for more than a week with FBA competition still heavy, you’re too far above breakeven to play that game. Plan in advance whether you’d (a) accept a small loss at a lower price, or (b) dump via other channels.

8. **Re-run with your exact fee inputs:** once you lock in the final FBA + referral + prep numbers, re-plug the breakeven; if breakeven turns out higher than 11.10, the tiny £24 gross becomes even thinner and you may prefer a **faster liquidation price** with minimal loss instead of chasing profit.

To keep it usable in text, I’m doing this:

Each Week N has 3 per-seller values:

Init = the original deterministic forecast weekly sales (from the Realistic 7-week table you pasted).

MC = the Monte Carlo mean weekly sales from the “INITIAL SNAPSHOT (first Keepa screenshots)” run.

Actual* = the observed average weekly sales in the second month, taken from the CURRENT SNAPSHOT hist_weekly_rate for overlapping sellers.

For sellers that do not appear in the second snapshot, Actual is N/A (we simply do not have their second-month rate).

For sellers that do appear, Actual is constant across weeks in this table, because the only thing we truly know is their “true 30-day” from the second batch, not a week-by-week breakdown.

All numbers are units sold per week (not stock levels).

═══ METHOD 2: FACTOR-WEIGHTED DETERMINISTIC MODEL ═══

ChatGPT said:
Thought for 1m 2s

Short answer: with the way we’ve currently implemented both, your factor-weighted deterministic model is more “accurate” as a central depletion forecast than the current Monte Carlo version. The Monte Carlo run is more useful as a risk envelope (ranges, probabilities), but it is less tightly anchored to the actual last-30-days seller behaviour because of how we coded it.

I’ll break that down concretely.

1. What Method 2 (factor-weighted deterministic) actually does

Method 2 is the one with:

Sales
week
=
(
HistWeeklyRate
×
𝑤
)
+
(
BuyBoxShare
×
MarketVolume
)
Sales
week
	​

=(HistWeeklyRate×w)+(BuyBoxShare×MarketVolume)

with

𝑤
=
30
%
w=30% in weeks 1–2, 
𝑤
=
20
%
w=20% afterwards

MarketVolume = MonthlySold ÷ 4.3

BuyBoxShare built from FBA / price / reviews / stock (65 / 22.5 / 5 / 8.5)

So each seller’s weekly sales is a sum of two pieces:

History piece:
HistWeeklyRate × w

Example: REVON true 30-day sales ≈ 90

HistWeeklyRate ≈ 90 / 4.3 ≈ 20.9 units/week

With w = 0.3 (week 1):
history part ≈ 20.9 × 0.3 ≈ 6.3 units

Buy Box piece:
BuyBoxShare × MarketVolume

Example: total market ≈ 300/month → ≈ 69.8 units/week

Suppose REVON’s BuyBoxShare in week 1 is 0.20 (20%) →
BB part ≈ 0.20 × 69.8 ≈ 14 units

Total week-1 sales forecast for REVON ≈ 6.3 + 14 ≈ 20.3 units
(then clamped by stock if needed).

So for every seller, Method 2:

Starts from what they really did over the last 30 days (HistWeeklyRate).

Adjusts upwards or downwards based on current FBA/FBM, price, reviews, stock through BuyBoxShare.

Runs this deterministically week by week, updating stock.

Result: one clean path per seller:

Week 1: “21 – 10.1 = 10.9”

Week 2: “10.9 – 10.9 = 0”

… etc.

This is why the “Realistic” 7-week table you pasted looks very structured and concentrated: the heavy movers stay heavy movers, OOS sellers stop, “parked” sellers remain slow.

2. What the Monte Carlo script actually does

The Monte Carlo version we wrote does something related, but not identical.

2.1. Demand

It sets a mean weekly market volume (e.g. 400 / 4.3 ≈ 93.0 units/week for the latest snapshot).

It converts that to daily mean and then draws daily demand from a Negative Binomial → demand bounces around each day.

Example:

Average daily demand ≈ 93.0 / 7 ≈ 13.3 units/day

Some days might be 5 units, some 20+, etc.

2.2. Daily Buy Box shares

For each day:

Look at which sellers still have stock.

Build a vector of Dirichlet α values using:

FBA vs FBM (65%)

price (22.5%)

reviews (5%)

stock (8.5%)

Crucial point:
This α vector uses only FBA, price, reviews, stock.
It does NOT use hist_weekly_rate at all.

So large-stock, high-review sellers with good pricing get a larger α, which means they tend to get more of the daily share in the simulation, even if in the last 30 days they actually sold very little.

Then, for each day:

Sample a BB share vector from a Dirichlet with that α.

Sample total demand for the day from the Negative Binomial.

Allocate that demand among sellers via a multinomial using the sampled BB shares.

Clamp by stock, reduce stocks accordingly, record sales and BB share.

Do that for 56 days × 10,000 iterations → then aggregate to weekly means and 95% ranges.

That is why you see things like:

The Online Stores (FBM), Hexses FBM, Shakti getting non-trivial mean weekly sales in phase-1 Monte Carlo, even though we classified them earlier as “parked” with ~1–3 real sales in 30 days.

Your comment that Monte Carlo “disperses” sales across more sellers is correct.
The Dirichlet construction forces every in-stock seller to receive some share in expectation.

3. How they differ in terms of accuracy
3.1. Data anchoring

Method 2 (deterministic):

Directly anchored to Phase-1 true 30-day sales per seller through HistWeeklyRate.

If REVON did 90 in 30 days and The Online Stores FBA did 2, that gap is built into the model.

The BB share term tweaks this based on current conditions but cannot fully “erase” that historical difference.

Monte Carlo (as implemented now):

Ignores HistWeeklyRate inside the share mechanism.

It only cares about FBA vs FBM, price, reviews, stock on that day.

That means it will often give too much demand to big-stock, high-review, but historically slow sellers (e.g. “parking” FBM listings), because nothing in α reminds it that these sellers barely sold anything last month.

So purely on “anchored to what actually happened in the last 30 days”,
Method 2 is more constrained by reality than the current Monte Carlo configuration.

3.2. Output type

Method 2:

One definitive trajectory per seller.

Very useful if you want a single playbook: “I expect to sell ~8, 16, 24, 12 and be out by end of week 4.”

Monte Carlo:

A cloud of trajectories (10,000 paths), from which we extract:

mean weekly sales

2.5% / 97.5% ranges

probability of selling out by week X

This is not automatically “more accurate”.
It’s richer and probabilistic, but the centre of that distribution (the mean) is only as good as the calibration of the model.

3.3. For your listing specifically (“ME”)

Deterministic Method 2 (Realistic) said:

W1 ~ 8

W2 ~ 16

W3 ~ 24

W4 ~ 12

Sell-out ≈ end of week 4

Monte Carlo (initial snapshot) gave (means):

W1 ≈ 9.37

W2 ≈ 14.41

W3 ≈ 17.17

W4 ≈ 12.66

W5 ≈ 5.13

W6 ≈ 1.12

… total = 60 over ~5 weeks on the mean path

P(sell-out by week 4) ≈ 54.7%

P(sell-out by week 5) ≈ 86.6%

So:

Method 2: “essentially guaranteed” week-4 sell-out in that scenario.

Monte Carlo: “about half the time week-4, a large chunk of the time week-5”.

Given we don’t have an exact day-by-day stock history for your own listing between the two screenshot dates, we cannot rigorously score which central prediction was closer. We know only that you did sell all 60 within roughly that horizon.

But structurally:

Both agree that 60 units at £11.50 clear fairly quickly (4–5 weeks).

The main disagreement is on the shape and the cross-seller distribution, not on the basic “is this safe” question.

4. So which one is “more accurate” in your context?

Given:

We trust the Phase-1 “true 30-day sales” audit.

We know some sellers have large parked stock but low real sell-through.

The current Monte Carlo ignores HistWeeklyRate inside the share mechanism and therefore over-spreads demand across sellers.

Then:

For a single central depletion forecast (per seller, per week):

Method 2 (factor-weighted deterministic) is more reliable.
It keeps sellers who actually sold 90 units in 30 days at a higher sustained rate than those who sold 1–3, and this flows into depletion timing.

For uncertainty and risk bounds:

Monte Carlo is more informative because it gives:

a distribution of sell-out weeks (e.g. 55% by week 4, 87% by week 5)

ranges of weekly sales, not just point values.

As Monte Carlo is currently coded, it is not a better central forecast than Method 2.

It is a probabilistic wrapper around a share model that is missing the HistWeeklyRate input. That’s why you see “dispersion” that doesn’t match what your eyeballing of Keepa suggests: too many weeks where slow, parked sellers get meaningful sales.

5. What this implies for how you should use them

Until we modify the Monte Carlo script to incorporate history per seller, the clean view is:

Use Method 2 as your main working forecast for:

which sellers will deplete when

how quickly you clear your 60 units under different scenarios

rough weekly sales per seller

Use Monte Carlo as a risk analysis layer:

“What is the chance I’m still sitting on stock in week 6?”

“What’s the 95% worst case for my units sold after 8 weeks?”

If you want Monte Carlo to be competitive as a central forecast, we need to change its internals so that each seller’s expected daily share matches their Phase-1 weekly rate more closely. Concretely, that means:

Either:

Add a HistWeeklyRate component into α in build_alpha_vector,
so that sellers with much higher recent sales get larger α even if their stock is big or reviews are high for others.

Or:

Calibrate a per-seller multiplier so that, if we simulate 30 days with the current parameters, the mean sales per seller over those 30 days matches the Phase-1 “true 30-day” numbers.

Once we do that, Monte Carlo’s mean paths will converge much closer to Method 2’s deterministic path, and its key advantage will be the probabilistic envelope, not a different central forecast.

If you want, next step I can outline exactly how to change build_alpha_vector (and/or add a calibration step) so that the Monte Carlo share pattern respects the Phase-1 true 30-day sales more tightly, and therefore your per-seller weekly patterns look less “spread out” and more like what you see on the stock graphs.
