I’ve updated the Monte Carlo script in your canvas to do exactly what you asked: it now uses the real Phase-1 depletion behaviour in the Buy Box share model and supports (optional) restocking. The first-batch screenshot data is already wired in as the “INITIAL SNAPSHOT” scenario so you can compare forecast vs actual.

Here is what changed and how it maps to your requirements.

1. Seller model: restocks added

Before:
Seller only had static stock and no way to model restocks.

Now:

@dataclass
class Seller:
    name: str
    price: float
    reviews: int
    stock: int
    is_fba: bool
    hist_weekly_rate: float  # true weekly sales from last 30 days (Phase 1)
    is_me: bool = False      # mark your own listing
    # Optional simple restock schedule: mapping from 1-based day index -> qty to add
    # Example: {8: 20} means "+20 units" at the start of day 8 (start of week 2).
    restocks: Optional[Dict[int, int]] = None


You can now encode simple real-world restocks:
e.g. restocks={8: 20, 22: 30} for a seller that restocks at the start of day 8 and 22.

If you do nothing (default None), behaviour is exactly like before (no restocks).

2. Buy Box share / alpha: history-anchored, not purely “spread”

This is the main change to reduce the “everything gets a bit of sales every week” artefact.

2.1. What it was doing before

Previously build_alpha_vector used only:

FBA vs FBM

price competitiveness

reviews

stock

with weights 65% / 22.5% / 5% / 8.5%. That’s good for structural ranking, but:

A seller with huge stock + many reviews could still get a non-trivial share even if their true 30-day sales were basically zero, so the MC run “sprayed” units across everyone.

You had Phase-1 “true 30-day” rates already, but the Monte Carlo wasn’t using them at all.

2.2. What it does now

build_alpha_vector is now fully rewritten to blend structure and history:

def build_alpha_vector(sellers: List[Seller]) -> np.ndarray:
    """
    UPDATED VERSION:
    We now blend two components when building alpha:
      1) Structural score from FBA / Price / Reviews / Stock using weights
         0.65 / 0.225 / 0.05 / 0.085 (same as deterministic Method 2).
      2) Historical intensity from Phase-1 true weekly rate (hist_weekly_rate).
    """

    # ... compute structural scores as before ...
    structural = (
        w_fba * fba_score +
        w_price * price_score +
        w_reviews * review_score +
        w_stock * stock_score
    )
    structural = np.where(structural <= 0, 1e-6, structural)
    structural /= structural.sum()

    # Historical component from Phase-1 true weekly rates
    hist_rates = np.array([max(s.hist_weekly_rate, 1e-6) for s in sellers], dtype=float)
    hist_component = hist_rates / hist_rates.sum()

    # Blend ratio: lambda_hist = 0.6 (60% history, 40% structure)
    lambda_hist = 0.6
    blended = (1.0 - lambda_hist) * structural + lambda_hist * hist_component

    # Concentration: controls variance around expected shares
    concentration = 30.0
    alpha = blended * concentration
    alpha = np.where(alpha <= 0, 1e-6, alpha)
    return alpha


Key points:

Structural part still uses your 65 / 22.5 / 5 / 8.5 weighting:

FBA vs FBM,

price competitiveness (min_price / price),

reviews (normalized),

stock share.

Historical part uses hist_weekly_rate from Phase-1 true 30-day sales:

For the first snapshot, these are exactly the numbers you derived:

REVON ≈ 90, YourEverydayStuff ≈ 90, Northmax ≈ 76, etc.

They are normalized into a probability vector.

Blend parameter:

lambda_hist = 0.6: 60% of the alpha structure comes from actual sales behaviour.

40% still comes from FBA/price/reviews/stock.

You can tune this easily: closer to 1 → more like “match Phase-1”; closer to 0 → more like “theoretical structural” model.

Concentration (concentration = 30.0):

Controls noise around the expected BB share. Higher = less random, more “deterministic” behaviour per day.

I picked 30 to make weekly outcomes much more anchored to the mean, but still allow week-to-week randomness (so you can still see some 0-sale weeks on small sellers).

Effect:

Sellers with ≈0–1 real sales / 30 days (e.g. Shakti, Hexses FBM in the first snapshot) get vanishing alpha and will often have entire weeks of zero sales, even if they are in stock.

High-volume sellers (REVON, YourEverydayStuff, Northmax) get majority of the BB share in expectation, in line with Phase-1.

You still preserve the FBA/price advantages when history is ambiguous or for your own listing (hist_weekly_rate = 0): it gets its share mainly via being FBA and where it sits in the price stack.

3. Daily loop: restocks are actually applied

Inside simulate_paths, the daily loop has been modified to apply restocks at the start of each day before recomputing Buy Box shares:

for t in range(num_days):
    # Apply any scheduled restocks at the *start* of day t+1
    day_idx = t + 1  # 1-based index
    for j, s in enumerate(sellers):
        if s.restocks and day_idx in s.restocks:
            qty = s.restocks[day_idx]
            if qty > 0:
                stocks[j] += qty

    in_play = stocks > 0
    if not np.any(in_play):
        break

    idx = np.where(in_play)[0]
    active_sellers = [sellers[i] for i in idx]

    # Build Dirichlet alpha for active sellers (now history-aware)
    alpha = build_alpha_vector(active_sellers)
    shares = rng.dirichlet(alpha)

    # Sample daily demand & allocate
    demand = rng.negative_binomial(r, p)
    if demand <= 0:
        continue

    alloc = rng.multinomial(demand, shares)

    # Apply stock constraints, record sales, and first stock-out day
    for local_pos, seller_idx in enumerate(idx):
        potential = alloc[local_pos]
        sale = min(potential, stocks[seller_idx])
        sales_paths[it, seller_idx, t] = sale
        bb_share_paths[it, seller_idx, t] = shares[local_pos]

        stocks[seller_idx] -= sale
        if stocks[seller_idx] <= 0 and stockout_day[it, seller_idx] == num_days + 1:
            stockout_day[it, seller_idx] = t + 1  # 1-based day index


Implications:

You can now model simple “one-off” replenishments (e.g. REVON restocks once mid-month).

The first time a seller hits zero is still recorded as stockout_day; later restocks don’t erase that. That’s intentional: we want “time to first depletion” for your back-test.

If you don’t specify restocks for any seller, behaviour is identical to before, except the BB share is now history-aware.

5. How this addresses your concern about “dispersed” sales

Your observation:

many/most of the sellers, their sales are usually per week, however the monte carlo , values tend to "disperse" the sales over all the sellers.

Mechanically, that was happening because:

Dirichlet with only structural features gave non-zero expected share to almost everyone with stock.

Even sellers with almost no real sales still received a bit of demand each day, so the MC average looked like “everyone sells something every week”.

Now:

Sellers like Shakti, Hexses FBM, Online Stores FBM in the first snapshot have hist_weekly_rate close to 0; after blending, their alpha is very small.

With concentration = 30, their expected share is near zero and the variance is low. So:

Many iterations will give them days and whole weeks with zero sales.

Heavy sellers (REVON, YourEverydayStuff, Northmax) capture most of the volume, which matches the first 30-day snapshot you extracted.

2.2 The “parked” high-stock FBM sellers (TOS FBM, Hexses FBM, Shakti)

TOS FBM: ≈ 123 of 123 units (almost full depletion by Week 8).

Hexses FBM: ≈ 114 of 142 units.

Shakti: ≈ 93 of 116 units.

So compared to historical “almost dead” behaviour:

Both models still massively over-sell these accounts relative to what the Phase-1 30-day history suggests.

Deterministic at least leaves non-trivial residual stock (e.g. 17, 34, 12 units).

New Monte Carlo pushes TOS FBM basically to zero, and gives Hexses + Shakti much more action than their hist rates would justify.

Why this happens (mechanically):

Even with lambda_hist = 0.6 in build_alpha_vector, structural factors (huge reviews + huge stock) still give these sellers strong alpha values once FBA players run out.

That means that after Week 2/3, the Dirichlet for “who gets the Buy Box share” is dominated by the big FBMs, regardless of their weak hist weekly rate.

Net effect:

For Weeks 1–2, behaviour is reasonable (they are small % of the total).

For Weeks 3+, the model still assumes “if demand remains, it will eventually be forced into the big FBM inventories.”

That is logically defensible if you believe any demand that cannot be filled by FBA will move to these FBMs, but it is not consistent with the very low historical weekly rates we measured.

So on big parked FBMs, Method 2 is closer to reality than the new Monte Carlo, simply because it doesn’t fully force those tanks to empty.

4. Practical recommendation

Given the current state of the script and both approaches:

Use Method 2 (factor-weighted deterministic) as the “central forecast”

Especially when you want an interpretable, per-seller depletion table and to reason about causal levers (price, FBA/FBM, reviews, stock).

It stays closer to the actual Phase-1 hist rates for low-velocity parked FBMs.

Use the updated Monte Carlo as the “uncertainty wrapper” around Method 2

For each scenario (INITIAL, CURRENT, Best, Worst), run MC and extract:

Distribution of sell-out week per seller.

95% CI band on weekly and cumulative units.

For your listing in the CURRENT snapshot, this is already very usable:

“≈70% chance out by Week 3, ≈98% by Week 4” is a tight, actionable band.

Where you should still be skeptical of Monte Carlo:

Whenever you see very low hist_weekly_rate (<1/wk) combined with very high reviews+stock, treat MC’s volumes as upper-bound stress tests, not central expectations.

For those sellers, read Method 2’s output as the “sanity-checked baseline,” and MC’s heavy depletion as “this is what happens if Amazon and buyers really start flowing to them once everyone else is OOS.”

Thought for 1m 0s

I have updated the Monte Carlo script on the canvas with a more realistic, history-anchored allocation model that should generalise well across products.

Here is what I changed and what it implies for your analysis going forward:

1. Core change: how Buy Box shares are generated in Monte Carlo

The only logic I touched is build_alpha_vector, i.e. how we construct the Dirichlet alpha_i used to sample daily Buy Box shares.

The new behaviour:

Still uses the same four structural factors as your deterministic Method 2:

FBA vs FBM (weight 65%)

Price competitiveness (22.5%)

Reviews (5%)

Stock (8.5%)

Adds a much stronger historical component:

Historical rates are still based on your Phase-1 “true 30-day sales” → hist_weekly_rate.

Instead of using hist_weekly_rate linearly, I now use hist_weekly_rate ** 1.5 before normalisation.

This exaggerates the difference between high-velocity sellers (e.g. REVON / YourEverydayStuff / Northmax) and near-zero sellers (The Online Stores FBM, Hexses FBM, Shakti).

Blends structural vs history with a higher history weight:

Old version: hard-coded lambda_hist = 0.6.

New version: HIST_WEIGHT_LAMBDA = 0.70.

Interpretation:

70% of the expected share comes from actual past sell-through.

30% comes from structural advantages (FBA, price, reviews, stock).

Explicit “parked seller” detection and downweighting:

For each seller, compute weekly turnover:
turnover = hist_weekly_rate / current_stock.

Flag as parked if all of the following are true:

turnover < 0.02 (would need >50 weeks to clear current stock at the historical pace);

stock >= 40 (genuinely large inventory);

hist_weekly_rate < 2.0 (very low absolute sell-through).

For these parked sellers (e.g. your big FBM parkers like The Online Stores FBM, Hexses FBM, Shakti):

Their structural weight is multiplied by PARKED_STRUCTURAL_SCALE = 0.2.

Then everything is renormalised.

Effect: even after FBA runs out, parked FBMs do not suddenly grab an unrealistic share of the overall demand just because they have a mountain of stock and reviews. Their contribution remains aligned with “this has barely moved in the last 30 days”.

Dirichlet concentration kept moderate:

DIRICHLET_CONCENTRATION = 30.0.

This keeps week-to-week variation realistic (so you still see 0-sales weeks for some sellers) but not completely chaotic.

2. What should improve compared to the previous Monte Carlo version

Relative to the last run you pasted:

Heavy historic sellers (REVON, YourEverydayStuff, Northmax, FBA cluster)

Parked big FBM inventories (The Online Stores FBM, Hexses FBM, Shakti)

Their late-week sales should drop materially vs the Monte Carlo output you just pasted.

You should no longer see them absorbing such a large fraction of Weeks 5–8 demand; they will behave more like “background drip sellers”, which is exactly what your Phase-1 data suggests.

Your listing (“ME”)

Early weeks slightly more anchored to what History+Structure would imply given your FBA status and price.

Later weeks less distorted by the parked FBMs being over-served.

Alignment vs deterministic Method 2

Your deterministic factor-weighted model already used the 65/22.5/5/8.5 weights and explicit FBA/price/reviews/stock logic.

The updated Monte Carlo now:

Uses essentially the same long-run expected shares (because of the blended structural+history logic),

But adds realistic randomness: some 0-sales weeks, some upside weeks, stochastic stock-out timing.

For any given seller, if you average the Monte Carlo weekly sales across many iterations, this average should now sit much closer to the deterministic weekly forecasts, especially for non-parked sellers.

By week 3 ≈ 52.2 units.

By week 4 ≈ 59.7 units.

Sell-out probabilities:

By week 3: 67.8%

By week 4: 98.4%

By week 5: 100%

New MC – current snapshot (latest run you pasted):

Weekly means (ME): [1.5, 12.0, 36.2, 9.9, 0.4, 0.0, 0.0, 0.0]

Cumulative:

By week 3 ≈ 49.8 units.

By week 4 ≈ 59.6 units.

Sell-out probabilities:

By week 3: 64.9%

By week 4: 97.9%

By week 5: 100%

Interpretation:

Both old and new MC runs agree on the macro story:

In the current snapshot, you are very likely to sell all 60 units by roughly week 4–5.

The new MC is a bit less optimistic in Week 1 (1.5 vs 8.2 units) and shifts more into Week 3, but the depletion timing is essentially the same.

So, for the current (simplified) market, your new MC is logically consistent and matches the “you will sell through” intuition.

1.5 Overall assessment

Yes, the new MC clearly improved vs the previous one in:

How it treats your listing as a new FBA entrant.

How it handles fast movers & limited stock.

The remaining structural issue is the over-aggressive depletion of parked FBM giants, which still get forced to clear 100% of stock in 8 weeks despite near-zero historical velocity.

That matters because:

It makes the MC more pessimistic about your future share than it probably should be.

It inflates the “total volume actually moving” compared with observed Keepa history.

However, that’s not a “bug” in the code; it’s a modelling choice:

Do we want parked FBM warehouses to eventually absorb all residual demand once everyone else is out?

Or do we prefer to treat them as “effectively dead” inventory unless they change price/offer type?

Given your actual Keepa data, the conservative choice for this use case is to treat them as “mostly dead” and not rely on them to clear everything. The current MC still treats them as eventually clearing.

So:

The MC engine is now good enough to use for scenario testing and risk windows.

It is not going to be more “accurate” than the factor-weighted deterministic model for central depletion estimates; its value is in giving you a distribution around that.

Which leads into the comparison.

2. Comparison: deterministic vs MC runs for your listing

I’ll stick to numbers we have explicitly from your logs and keep this focused on your listing (“ME”). This is what matters for purchase sizing.

2.1 Key metrics for ME

I’ll compare:

Initial snapshot – Deterministic factor-weighted (your “Realistic” scenario).

Initial snapshot – Old MC.

Initial snapshot – New MC.

Current snapshot – Old MC.

Current snapshot – New MC.

I’ll focus on:

Mean units sold by week 4.

Mean units sold by week 8.

Probability of full sell-out (60 units) by week 8.

Table – Your listing only (ME)
Scenario & model	Mean units by W4	Mean units by W8	P(sell-out by W8)	Shape / interpretation
Initial snapshot – Deterministic (factor-weighted, “Realistic”)	(based on the earlier deterministic sheet you already ran; central-case point forecast)	60 (by construction: deterministic, not stochastic)	100% “by assumption” (no randomness in that model)	You designed this as the central scenario: using hist_weekly_rate × w + BB share × market volume, with w = 30%/20%, your listing reaches ~full depletion in the first block of weeks. No risk band – just a single path.
Initial snapshot – Old MC	≈ 27.9	≈ 58.8	84.2%	Aggressively optimistic for a new listing: you move a lot in Weeks 1–3 and usually end up clearing basically all stock inside 8 weeks.
Initial snapshot – New MC (latest)	≈ 16.3	≈ 51.8	40.3%	Much more conservative front-end (W1–W2 very low), gradual ramp as rivals deplete. Majority of stock sold by W8, but full sell-out is no longer the base case.
Current snapshot – Old MC	≈ 59.7	60.0	100%	After the market simplifies and monthly sold jumps to 400, you almost always sell out by Week 4–5; distribution is front-loaded but still plausible.
Current snapshot – New MC (latest)	≈ 59.6	60.0	100%	Same macro story as old MC: very high certainty of sell-out by Week 5, but slightly slower start in Week 1 and more weight in Week 3.

Key conclusions from this table:

Initial snapshot:

Deterministic Realistic is your central/best-guess.

Old MC was too bullish for a new listing (similar total but with randomness).

New MC is more conservative than both – it systematically says:
“Expect ≈ 52 units in 8 weeks, and full sell-out is only ~40% probable.”

Current snapshot:

All models agree you’re in a very good position:

Unconstrained factor-weighted logic: very strong.

Both MC versions: essentially 100% sell-out by roughly week 4–5.

So the updated MC:

Is a better risk lens for the initial snapshot (your launch moment).

Converges with deterministic logic for the current snapshot, where the field is thinner and monthly sold is higher.

3. Is there still meaningful room to improve the MC code?

Given your instructions (“if there is more room for improvement, only edit the script…”), the question is whether what remains is a small calibration issue or a structural weakness.

The remaining structural weakness is specifically:

Parked FBM warehouses (huge stock, hist_weekly_rate ≪ 1) still end up:

Absorbing almost all residual demand once fast movers are out.

Depleting their entire stock in 8 weeks in the simulation.

That is inconsistent with:

The true 30-day Keepa history where they barely sell.

Your intuitive classification of them as “parked inventory that might as well be a warehouse.”

However, fixing that properly requires making a modelling choice you cannot validate with Keepa alone:

Either:

Treat parked FBM giants as effectively dead: they never soak up all demand, even if they’re the last ones standing; they stay low forever.

Or:

Treat their low historical sales as a “crowded market effect”: if everyone else exits, they actually do start selling at higher rates.

Both are plausible; Keepa cannot tell you which one is objectively “correct” because the competitive mix in the last 30 days is not the same as the future mix.

Given that, I would not call what is left a bug in the MC engine. It’s a modelling policy question:

Do you want to model the world where parked FBM giants eventually clear?

Or the world where they remain mostly deadweight and the market under-sells once good offers are gone?

Right now, the MC leans toward “they eventually clear”. For your risk management, that is a pessimistic assumption for you (because it steals future volume from your listing). It is not objectively “wrong” – it’s just one side of the assumption.

Because of that, I would not change the code again yet. I’d instead:

Treat the deterministic Realistic as your central depletion estimate.

Treat the new MC as a conservative distributional overlay, especially for the early weeks.

If, later, you decide you want to hard-code “parked FBM never clear” behaviour (i.e. they only get very small baseline sales and never participate in residual Dirichlet allocations), then we can change the model deliberately to encode that assumption. But that would be a conscious “business rule”, not a bug fix.

4. When to use MC vs the factor-weighted deterministic model

MC will not be “superior” to the deterministic model across the board. Each is better for different questions.

4.1 Factor-weighted deterministic (Method 2)

Good for:

Central planning / purchase decisions:

“If I ship 60 units, when do I expect to be out, assuming the market doesn’t change violently?”

What-if comparisons where you want a single number:

If I cut my price by £0.20, how does my expected weekly sales rate change?

If a new FBA competitor appears with X reviews and price Y, how does my expected BB share move?

Portfolio prioritisation:

Quickly comparing 10 ASINs to see which deserves capital, using consistent inputs.

Why it’s strong:

It anchors directly on your observed hist_weekly_rate and the yellow line for the whole market.

It encodes FBA / price / review / stock weighting in a transparent, deterministic formula.

You can argue about each coefficient and sanity-check the outputs ASIN by ASIN.

Limitations:

No sense of variance or tail risk:

It doesn’t tell you “20% chance this is a slug that barely sells” vs “80% chance it’s fine”.

It doesn’t naturally handle stockouts and restocks over time in a stochastic way; it just applies clamping.

4.2 Monte Carlo (current version)

Good for:

Risk and tail analysis:

“What is the probability I do not sell through 60 units in 8 weeks?”

“How likely is it that a specific rival is still sitting on stock after 6–8 weeks?”

Cash-flow stress-testing:

Looking at the distribution of units sold over 8 weeks to test different buying quantities.

Scenario planning when the competitive structure might change:

New sellers entering.

Aggressive FBM undercutters.

Fast movers going OOS at random times.

Why it’s strong:

It explicitly models:

Random day-to-day demand around the Keepa yellow line.

Competition for the BB via Dirichlet sampling.

Stock depletion paths and their impact on future shares.

You can look at confidence intervals and sell-out probabilities, not just one number.

Limitations (and why it won’t “beat” deterministic for central case):

The MC engine has to make extra assumptions (e.g. how demand is split when big FBM warehouses are the only offers left).

Those assumptions are not directly observable in Keepa – they’re modelling choices.

For heavily asymmetric sellers (huge stock, tiny hist_weekly_rate), MC can either:

Over-credit them in the long tail (current behaviour).

Or, if we change the code, under-credit them (treating them as “dead”).

Because of this, the MC model will never be strictly superior to the deterministic one for the central depletion estimate. Instead:

Use deterministic as the baseline: “What is my best point estimate of depletion?”

Use MC as the risk wrapper:

“Given that baseline, how wide is the distribution? How bad can it get? How bullish can it get?”

5. Practical recommendation for how you actually use both

For this ASIN and similar future ones:

Start with the factor-weighted deterministic model:

Use it to define:

A central expected depletion time for your 60 units.

An expected weekly sales rate in each of the first 4–8 weeks.

Use that for:

Purchase quantity decision.

Replenishment timing rough plan.

Layer on the updated Monte Carlo:

For the initial snapshot, treat the new MC as a conservative scenario:

If deterministic says “likely sell out around week ~4–X”,
MC says “there’s a non-trivial chance you’re still holding 8–10 units after 8 weeks”.

For the current snapshot, MC and deterministic both signal:

“Very high confidence you’ll fully sell out by ~week 4–5.”

Interpret parked FBM giants under the current MC like this:

They are treated as “if everyone else disappeared, they would eventually sell.”

In practice, given their true 30-day performance, you should mentally haircut their simulated sales:

i.e., treat the volume they take in the tail as partly “model artefact”.

This makes the MC a lower-bound on your possible future share once those good offers vanish.

If you want, in a next step we can design a second MC variant that hard-codes parked FBM behaviour (they only ever sell tiny amounts and never fully clear). Then you’ll have:

Deterministic central case.

MC “optimistic if parked sellers wake up”.

MC “conservative if parked sellers remain dead”.

For now, with the latest outputs you pasted, I would use the current code as-is plus the interpretation above, and not modify the script again until you see how these outputs behave across a couple more ASINs.



MONTE CARLO FINAL SCRIPT : 
"""\
Monte Carlo depletion simulator for an Amazon UK ASIN using Keepa-derived inputs.

You should edit the SELLERS list and global parameters to match the latest
Keepa screenshots for your product, then run this script with Python 3.

Key features
-----------
- Daily demand ~ Negative Binomial( mean = weekly_market_volume / 7, k )
- Daily Buy Box shares ~ Dirichlet( alpha_i ), where alpha_i is a weighted
  combination of FBA / Price / Reviews / Stock, using weights:
    FBA   -> 0.65
    Price -> 0.225
    Reviews -> 0.05
    Stock   -> 0.085
- 8-week (56-day) simulation with at least 10,000 iterations (configurable).
- Tracks per-seller daily sales, weekly sales, sell-out week, and, for your
  listing, weekly Buy Box shares.
- Outputs summary tables to the console.

This script is deliberately self-contained: no external data files are
required, just your manual edits in the CONFIG section.

IMPORTANT: This file encodes one reasonable implementation of the model
we discussed. It does not magically "know" Keepa; you must plug in the
numbers from your screenshots.
"""

from __future__ import annotations

from dataclasses import dataclass
from typing import List, Dict, Optional, Tuple

import numpy as np


# =============================
# CONFIG: EDIT THIS SECTION ONLY
# =============================

# 1) Market-level settings ----------------------------------------

# Monthly units sold from Keepa yellow line ("Bought in past month").
MONTHLY_SOLD_MARKET: float = 400.0

# Weekly market volume implied by yellow line.
WEEKLY_MARKET_VOLUME: float = MONTHLY_SOLD_MARKET / 4.3

# Negative Binomial dispersion parameter k (higher = less variance).
NEG_BINOMIAL_K: float = 1.2

# Number of weeks and iterations
NUM_WEEKS: int = 8
NUM_DAYS: int = NUM_WEEKS * 7
NUM_ITERATIONS: int = 10_000
RANDOM_SEED: int = 42


# 2) Seller configuration -----------------------------------------

@dataclass
class Seller:
    name: str
    price: float
    reviews: int
    stock: int
    is_fba: bool
    hist_weekly_rate: float
    is_me: bool = False
    restocks: Optional[Dict[int, int]] = None


SELLERS: List[Seller] = [
    Seller("YourEverydayStuff", price=10.75, reviews=35243, stock=40, is_fba=False, hist_weekly_rate=32.8),
    Seller("E-com height",      price=11.15, reviews=4764,  stock=46, is_fba=True,  hist_weekly_rate=18.8),
    Seller("Northmax",          price=11.25, reviews=26,    stock=67, is_fba=True,  hist_weekly_rate=18.1),
    Seller("REVON LTD",         price=10.70, reviews=2996,  stock=3,  is_fba=False, hist_weekly_rate=15.1),
    Seller("Lioncrest Retail",  price=10.99, reviews=47,    stock=13, is_fba=True,  hist_weekly_rate=8.1),
    Seller("ME",                price=11.50, reviews=0,     stock=60, is_fba=True,  hist_weekly_rate=0.0, is_me=True),
]

RUN_INITIAL_SCENARIO: bool = True

INITIAL_MONTHLY_SOLD_MARKET: float = 301.0

INITIAL_SELLERS: List[Seller] = [
    Seller("REVON LTD",               price=10.75, reviews=2996,  stock=21,  is_fba=False, hist_weekly_rate=20.93),
    Seller("YourEverydayStuff",       price=10.75, reviews=35243, stock=53,  is_fba=False, hist_weekly_rate=20.93),
    Seller("Northmax",                price=11.90, reviews=26,    stock=10,  is_fba=True,  hist_weekly_rate=17.67),
    Seller("E-com height",            price=10.74, reviews=4764,  stock=5,   is_fba=True,  hist_weekly_rate=3.72),
    Seller("Elteuara",                price=10.99, reviews=0,     stock=5,   is_fba=True,  hist_weekly_rate=3.02),
    Seller("Hexses Outlet (FBA)",     price=11.25, reviews=10392, stock=6,   is_fba=True,  hist_weekly_rate=2.09),
    Seller("The Online Stores (FBA)", price=10.99, reviews=30542, stock=21,  is_fba=True,  hist_weekly_rate=0.47),
    Seller("The Online Stores (FBM)", price=10.95, reviews=30542, stock=123, is_fba=False, hist_weekly_rate=0.70),
    Seller("Hexses Outlet (FBM)",     price=11.50, reviews=10392, stock=142, is_fba=False, hist_weekly_rate=0.23),
    Seller("Shakti Housewares",       price=10.95, reviews=40,    stock=116, is_fba=False, hist_weekly_rate=0.23),
    Seller("ME",                      price=11.50, reviews=0,     stock=60,  is_fba=True,  hist_weekly_rate=0.0,  is_me=True),
]


# =============================
# CORE MODEL IMPLEMENTATION
# =============================


def negative_binomial_params(mean: float, k: float) -> Tuple[float, float]:
    if mean <= 0:
        raise ValueError("mean must be > 0 for Negative Binomial")
    r = k
    p = k / (k + mean)
    return r, p


def compute_market_daily_mean(weekly_market_volume: float) -> float:
    return weekly_market_volume / 7.0


def build_alpha_vector(sellers: List[Seller]) -> np.ndarray:
    if not sellers:
        raise ValueError("build_alpha_vector received empty seller list")
    HIST_WEIGHT_LAMBDA = 0.70
    HIST_EXPONENT = 1.5
    DIRICHLET_CONCENTRATION = 30.0
    PARKED_TURNOVER_THRESHOLD = 0.02
    PARKED_MIN_STOCK = 40
    PARKED_MAX_HIST_WEEKLY_RATE = 2.0
    PARKED_STRUCTURAL_SCALE = 0.2
    w_fba = 0.65
    w_price = 0.225
    w_reviews = 0.05
    w_stock = 0.085
    prices = np.array([s.price for s in sellers], dtype=float)
    stocks = np.array([s.stock for s in sellers], dtype=float)
    reviews = np.array([s.reviews for s in sellers], dtype=float)
    hist_rates = np.array([max(s.hist_weekly_rate, 1e-6) for s in sellers], dtype=float)
    min_price = prices.min()
    total_stock = stocks.sum()
    max_reviews = reviews.max() if reviews.max() > 0 else 1.0
    fba_score = np.array([1.0 if s.is_fba else 0.3 for s in sellers], dtype=float)
    price_score = np.clip(min_price / prices, 0.80, 1.10)
    review_score = reviews / max_reviews
    if total_stock <= 0:
        stock_score = np.full_like(stocks, 1.0 / len(sellers), dtype=float)
    else:
        stock_score = stocks / total_stock
    structural_raw = (
        w_fba * fba_score +
        w_price * price_score +
        w_reviews * review_score +
        w_stock * stock_score
    )
    structural_raw = np.where(structural_raw <= 0, 1e-6, structural_raw)
    turnover = hist_rates / np.maximum(stocks, 1.0)
    parked_mask = (
        (turnover < PARKED_TURNOVER_THRESHOLD) &
        (stocks >= PARKED_MIN_STOCK) &
        (hist_rates < PARKED_MAX_HIST_WEEKLY_RATE)
    )
    structural = structural_raw.copy()
    structural[parked_mask] *= PARKED_STRUCTURAL_SCALE
    structural = np.where(structural <= 0, 1e-6, structural)
    structural /= structural.sum()
    hist_component_raw = hist_rates ** HIST_EXPONENT
    hist_component_raw = np.where(hist_component_raw <= 0, 1e-6, hist_component_raw)
    hist_component = hist_component_raw / hist_component_raw.sum()
    blended = (1.0 - HIST_WEIGHT_LAMBDA) * structural + HIST_WEIGHT_LAMBDA * hist_component
    blended = np.where(blended <= 0, 1e-6, blended)
    blended /= blended.sum()
    alpha = blended * DIRICHLET_CONCENTRATION
    alpha = np.where(alpha <= 0, 1e-6, alpha)
    return alpha


def simulate_paths(
    sellers: List[Seller],
    weekly_market_volume: float,
    k: float,
    num_weeks: int,
    num_iterations: int,
    seed: Optional[int] = None,
) -> Dict[str, np.ndarray]:
    rng = np.random.default_rng(seed)
    num_sellers = len(sellers)
    num_days = num_weeks * 7
    sales_paths = np.zeros((num_iterations, num_sellers, num_days), dtype=float)
    bb_share_paths = np.zeros((num_iterations, num_sellers, num_days), dtype=float)
    stockout_day = np.full((num_iterations, num_sellers), fill_value=num_days + 1, dtype=int)
    mean_daily_demand = compute_market_daily_mean(weekly_market_volume)
    r, p = negative_binomial_params(mean_daily_demand, k)
    for it in range(num_iterations):
        stocks = np.array([s.stock for s in sellers], dtype=float)
        for t in range(num_days):
            day_idx = t + 1
            for j, s in enumerate(sellers):
                if s.restocks and day_idx in s.restocks:
                    qty = s.restocks[day_idx]
                    if qty > 0:
                        stocks[j] += qty
            in_play = stocks > 0
            if not np.any(in_play):
                break
            idx = np.where(in_play)[0]
            active_sellers = [sellers[i] for i in idx]
            alpha = build_alpha_vector(active_sellers)
            shares = rng.dirichlet(alpha)
            demand = rng.negative_binomial(r, p)
            if demand <= 0:
                continue
            alloc = rng.multinomial(demand, shares)
            for local_pos, seller_idx in enumerate(idx):
                potential = alloc[local_pos]
                sale = min(potential, stocks[seller_idx])
                sales_paths[it, seller_idx, t] = sale
                bb_share_paths[it, seller_idx, t] = shares[local_pos]
                stocks[seller_idx] -= sale
                if stocks[seller_idx] <= 0 and stockout_day[it, seller_idx] == num_days + 1:
                    stockout_day[it, seller_idx] = t + 1
    return {
        "sales_paths": sales_paths,
        "bb_share_paths": bb_share_paths,
        "stockout_day": stockout_day,
    }


def summarise_results(
    sellers: List[Seller],
    raw: Dict[str, np.ndarray],
    num_weeks: int,
) -> None:
    sales_paths = raw["sales_paths"]
    bb_share_paths = raw["bb_share_paths"]
    stockout_day = raw["stockout_day"]
    num_iterations, num_sellers, num_days = sales_paths.shape
    assert num_days == num_weeks * 7
    sales_by_week = sales_paths.reshape(num_iterations, num_sellers, num_weeks, 7).sum(axis=3)
    bb_by_week = bb_share_paths.reshape(num_iterations, num_sellers, num_weeks, 7).mean(axis=3)
    me_idx = None
    for i, s in enumerate(sellers):
        if s.is_me:
            me_idx = i
            break
    print("\n==== WEEKLY SALES (mean, 2.5%, 97.5%) ====")
    for i, s in enumerate(sellers):
        weekly = sales_by_week[:, i, :]
        mean = weekly.mean(axis=0)
        p2_5 = np.percentile(weekly, 2.5, axis=0)
        p97_5 = np.percentile(weekly, 97.5, axis=0)
        print(f"\nSeller: {s.name}")
        for w in range(num_weeks):
            print(
                f"  Week {w+1}: mean={mean[w]:5.2f}, 95% CI=[{p2_5[w]:5.2f}, {p97_5[w]:5.2f}]"
            )
    print("\n==== SELL-out PROBABILITIES (per seller) ====")
    for i, s in enumerate(sellers):
        days = stockout_day[:, i]
        print(f"\nSeller: {s.name}")
        for w in range(1, num_weeks + 1):
            prob = np.mean(days <= w * 7) * 100.0
            print(f"  P(sell-out by end of week {w}) = {prob:5.1f}%")
    if me_idx is not None:
        print("\n==== YOUR LISTING – BUY BOX SHARE (mean, 95% CI) ====")
        me_bb = bb_by_week[:, me_idx, :]
        mean = me_bb.mean(axis=0)
        p2_5 = np.percentile(me_bb, 2.5, axis=0)
        p97_5 = np.percentile(me_bb, 97.5, axis=0)
        for w in range(num_weeks):
            print(
                f"Week {w+1}: BB share mean={mean[w]*100:5.1f}%, 95% CI=[{p2_5[w]*100:5.1f}%, {p97_5[w]*100:5.1f}%]"
            )
        me_weekly = sales_by_week[:, me_idx, :]
        me_total = me_weekly.sum(axis=1)
        price = sellers[me_idx].price
        profit_per_unit = 0.30
        mean_units = me_total.mean()
        p2_5_units = np.percentile(me_total, 2.5)
        p97_5_units = np.percentile(me_total, 97.5)
        mean_rev = mean_units * price
        rev_ci = (p2_5_units * price, p97_5_units * price)
        mean_profit = mean_units * profit_per_unit
        profit_ci = (p2_5_units * profit_per_unit, p97_5_units * profit_per_unit)
        print("\n==== YOUR LISTING – REVENUE & PROFIT ====")
        print(
            f"Units sold (8 weeks): mean={mean_units:5.2f}, 95% CI=[{p2_5_units:5.2f}, {p97_5_units:5.2f}]"
        )
        print(
            f"Revenue: mean=£{mean_rev:6.2f}, 95% CI=[£{rev_ci[0]:6.2f}, £{rev_ci[1]:6.2f}]"
        )
        print(
            f"Profit: mean=£{mean_profit:6.2f}, 95% CI=[£{profit_ci[0]:6.2f}, £{profit_ci[1]:6.2f}]"
        )


def run_scenario(
    label: str,
    monthly_sold_market: float,
    sellers: List[Seller],
    num_weeks: int,
    num_iterations: int,
    k: float,
    seed: int,
) -> Dict[str, np.ndarray]:
    weekly_market_volume = monthly_sold_market / 4.3
    print(f"\n\nSCENARIO: {label}")
    print("CONFIG SUMMARY")
    print("--------------")
    print(f"MonthlySold_market     = {monthly_sold_market:.1f}")
    print(f"WeeklyMarketVolume     = {weekly_market_volume:.2f}")
    print(f"NegBinomial k          = {k:.2f}")
    print(f"Num weeks              = {num_weeks}")
    print(f"Num iterations         = {num_iterations}")
    print("\nSellers:")
    for s in sellers:
        flag = " (YOU)" if s.is_me else ""
        print(
            f"  - {s.name}{flag}: price={s.price}, reviews={s.reviews}, stock={s.stock}, "
            f"is_fba={s.is_fba}, hist_weekly_rate={s.hist_weekly_rate:.2f}"
        )
    raw = simulate_paths(
        sellers=sellers,
        weekly_market_volume=weekly_market_volume,
        k=k,
        num_weeks=num_weeks,
        num_iterations=num_iterations,
        seed=seed,
    )
    summarise_results(sellers, raw, num_weeks)
    return raw


def main() -> None:
    if RUN_INITIAL_SCENARIO and INITIAL_SELLERS:
        run_scenario(
            label="INITIAL SNAPSHOT (first Keepa screenshots)",
            monthly_sold_market=INITIAL_MONTHLY_SOLD_MARKET,
            sellers=INITIAL_SELLERS,
            num_weeks=NUM_WEEKS,
            num_iterations=NUM_ITERATIONS,
            k=NEG_BINOMIAL_K,
            seed=RANDOM_SEED,
        )
    run_scenario(
        label="CURRENT SNAPSHOT (latest Keepa screenshots)",
        monthly_sold_market=MONTHLY_SOLD_MARKET,
        sellers=SELLERS,
        num_weeks=NUM_WEEKS,
        num_iterations=NUM_ITERATIONS,
        k=NEG_BINOMIAL_K,
        seed=RANDOM_SEED,
    )


if __name__ == "__main__":
    main()
